The Musharrakah Case: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Full Sibling Exclusion
Ruling on the case of Musharrakah and rebuttal of al-Shafi'i's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling in the Musharrakah case to calculate inheritance shares and argue against the Shafi'i position using the Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
When fixed shares exhaust the estate, full siblings inherit nothing—even if they are closer agnates.

This lesson unpacks the famous Musharrakah case in Hanafi faraid, where a specific set of heirs leads to a conflict between schools. You will learn the Hanafi ruling, its proof from the Quran and hadith, and the Shafi'i alternative. Understanding this dispute sharpens your skill in applying fixed shares and recognizing when 'asabah are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the family composition
List all heirs present: a husband, a mother (or grandmother), uterine siblings (ikhwah li-umm), and full siblings (ikhwah li-ab wa umm). These four categories are all present.
The text explicitly mentions 'a woman leaves a husband, mother or grandmother, uterine siblings, and full siblings'.
2
Assign fixed shares
Apply the Quranic shares: husband gets 1/2 (4:12), mother gets 1/6 (4:11), uterine siblings collectively get 1/3 (4:12). Sum these: 1/2 + 1/6 + 1/3 = 1. The entire estate is consumed.
No share is left for the full siblings as 'asabah.
3
Determine the consequence for full siblings
Since the fixed shares total 1, there is no residue. The hadith 'What the fixed shares leave, then to the nearest male agnate' is inapplicable. Therefore, full siblings inherit nothing (hajb hirman by exhaustion of estate). Contrast with al-Shafi'i who shares the 1/3 among all siblings.
The Hanafi rebuttal rests on the Quran specifying 'walad al-umm' for the 1/3 share, excluding full siblings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمُشَرَّكةُ : أن تترك المرأة زوجاً، وأُمَّـا أو جدة، وإخـوة من أم، وإخوةً من أب وأم : فللزوج : النصفُ، وللأم : السدس، ولولد الأم : الثلث. ولا شيء للإخوة من الأب والأم .
*
قوله: (والمُشركة : أن تترك المرأة زوجاً، وأُماً أو جدة، وإخوةً من أم، وإخوةً من أب وأم : فللزوج النصف، وللام : السدس، ولولد الأم: الثلث، ولا شيء للإخوة من الأب والأم).
وقال الشافعي" (۱): الثلث بين الإخوة للأم والإخوة للأب والأم بالسوية.
لنا: أن الله تعالى جَعَلَ للزوج النصف، وللأم السدس، وللإخوة من الأم الثلث، فاستغرقت الفريضة.
وقد قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما أبقت الفرائض: فَلأُولى عَصَبَةٍ ذكر (٢)، ولم يبق لهم شيء، والله أعلم.
And al-Musharrakah: that a woman leaves a husband, a mother or a grandmother, uterine siblings, and full siblings (from father and mother): for the husband is half, for the mother is one-sixth, for the uterine siblings is one-third. And nothing for the full siblings. * His saying: (al-Musharrakah: that a woman leaves a husband, a mother or a grandmother, uterine siblings, and full siblings: for the husband half, for the mother one-sixth, for the uterine children one-third, and nothing for the full siblings). And al-Shafi'i said (1): the one-third is between the uterine siblings and the full siblings equally. Our proof: that Allah the Exalted has decreed for the husband half, for the mother one-sixth, and for the uterine siblings one-third, so the estate is exhausted. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'What the fixed shares leave, then to the nearest male agnate' (2), and there remains nothing for them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the famous faraid case known as al-Musharrakah (the shared case). The scenario: a woman dies leaving a husband, a mother (or grandmother), uterine siblings (brothers and/or sisters from the mother's side), and full siblings (brothers and/or sisters from both parents). According to the Hanafi school: the husband takes half (1/2), the mother takes one-sixth (1/6), and the uterine siblings take one-third (1/3) collectively. Since these three fixed shares total exactly the whole estate (1/2 + 1/6 + 1/3 = 1), there is no residue left for the full siblings; they are totally excluded (hajb hirman). Imam al-Shafi'i holds a different opinion: he shares the one-third allotted to the uterine siblings with the full siblings, giving them equal shares (the 1/3 is divided equally among all siblings, both uterine and full). The Hanafi response (lana) rests on two proofs. First, the Quranic allocations: Allah gave specific shares to the husband, mother, and uterine siblings (Surah al-Nisa', 4:12, 4:11). These three shares add up to the whole estate, leaving nothing for the full siblings. Second, the hadith: 'What the fixed shares leave, then to the nearest male agnate' — but since the fixed shares consumed everything, there is no residue to pass to agnates. The full siblings are agnates, but they receive nothing because there is nothing left. The Hanafi position is that the full siblings are excluded because the estate is fully distributed. The dispute is recorded in many faraid works.

Key Terms
المُشَرَّكة (al-Musharrakah): The 'shared' case where uterine siblings and full siblings dispute the one-third share.
إخوة لأم (ikhwatun li-umm): Uterine siblings: those sharing only the mother. They are Quranic heirs entitled to 1/6 each (if alone) or 1/3 collectively.
إخوة لأب وأم (ikhwatun li-ab wa umm): Full siblings: those sharing both parents. They are 'asabah (agnates) by default.
عصبة ذكر ('asabah dhakar): A male agnate who inherits the residue after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Musharrakah case, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding full siblings?
A) They share the one-third with uterine siblings equally.
B) They inherit the residue after fixed shares.
C) They are totally excluded and receive nothing.
D) They inherit one-sixth each.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi Musharrakah ruling exemplifies that fixed shares (fara'id) take priority over 'asabah, and when they exhaust the estate, agnates inherit nothing.

Exclusion of Full Siblings When Fixed Shares Exhaust the Estate
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i on sibling share division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that full siblings inherit nothing when fixed shares exhaust the estate and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Fixed shares exhaust the estate; residuary heirs inherit nothing – the Hanafi rule for sibling exclusion.

In inheritance law, the presence of certain fixed shares can completely exclude residuary heirs. This passage presents a classic dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam al-Shafi'i over the distribution of the one-third share for siblings when the deceased leaves a husband, mother, and siblings. The Hanafi school argues that the one-third is exclusively for uterine siblings, leaving full siblings as residuary heirs who receive nothing because the estate is fully assigned. Let's examine the proofs and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed share
The deceased leaves a husband, a mother, and siblings — some uterine (only from mother) and some full (from both parents). The one-third share for siblings is at stake.
Uterine siblings are those sharing only the mother; full siblings share both parents.
2
State the two positions
Al-Shafi'i holds that the one-third is divided equally among all siblings (uterine and full). Abu Hanifa holds that the one-third is only for uterine siblings; full siblings are residuary heirs.
The Hanafi position is the one being defended in the passage.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
The Quran gives fixed shares: husband 1/2, mother 1/6, uterine siblings 1/3. These sum to 1, exhausting the estate. The hadith says the residue goes to the nearest male agnate, but there is no residue.
The hadith is quoted: 'ما أبقت الفرائض: فلأولى عصبة ذكر'.
4
Explain the consequence for full siblings
Since the estate is fully consumed by fixed shares, full siblings (who are residuary heirs) receive nothing. This is called hajb (exclusion) due to exhaustion.
Hajb here is not by a specific heir but by the shares themselves.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Hanafi law, full siblings inherit nothing in this scenario; under Shafi'i, they share the one-third equally with uterine siblings. This affects actual inheritance distribution.
The dispute highlights how a single verse interpretation can change outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحجب
وقال الشافعي: الثلث بين الإخوة للأم والإخوة للأب والأم بالسوية.
لنا: أن الله تعالى جعل للزوج النصف، وللأم السدس، وللإخوة من الأم الثلث، فاستغرقت الفريضة.
وقد قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما أبقت الفرائض: فلأولى عصبة ذكر، ولم يبق لهم شيء، والله أعلم.
Chapter of Hajb. Al-Shafi'i said: The third is between the siblings from mother and siblings from father and mother equally. Our proof: Allah made for the husband half, for the mother one-sixth, and for siblings from mother one-third, so the estate is exhausted. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'What the fixed shares leave is for the nearest male agnate,' and nothing remains for them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding inheritance distribution when there are siblings from both the mother (uterine) and siblings from both parents (full siblings). Al-Shafi'i holds that the one-third share for siblings from the mother is divided equally among all siblings (both uterine and full). The Hanafi school, represented by Abu Hanifa, objects: the fixed shares (husband gets half, mother gets one-sixth, uterine siblings get one-third) already sum to the whole estate, leaving no residue. The Hanafi proof includes the Quranic allocations and the hadith that the residue goes to the nearest male agnate; since nothing remains, the full siblings get nothing. The word 'الحجب' here refers to the exclusion of the full siblings because the estate is exhausted by the fixed shares.

Key Terms
الحجب (al-hajb): Exclusion of a residuary heir due to exhaustion of estate by fixed shares.
العصبة (al-‘asabah): Residuary heirs who take the remainder after fixed shares; here, full siblings were expected to be ‘asabah.
الفريضة (al-farīḍah): The fixed share of inheritance prescribed by the Quran (e.g., half, sixth, third).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a deceased leaves a husband, a mother, and uterine siblings (only from mother), what happens to the inheritance share of full siblings (from father and mother)?
A) They share equally with uterine siblings in the one-third share.
B) They get nothing because the fixed shares exhaust the estate.
C) They get the residue after all fixed shares are distributed.
D) They inherit as residuary heirs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, fixed shares exhaust the estate, excluding residuary heirs like full siblings.

Ruling on Return of Surplus (Radd) Excluding Spouses
Hanafi position on radd with Shafi'i contrast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on radd, identify that spouses are excluded, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Surplus returns to blood relatives, not spouses, because blood ties survive death while marriage ends.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule of radd—the return of surplus inheritance to fixed-share heirs when no residuary heir exists. The key issue is which heirs benefit from this surplus. The Hanafi school includes all blood relatives but excludes spouses, a point of dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the rule: the surplus after fixed shares, if no 'asabah, is returned to the fixed-share heirs except the spouses.
Spouses are excluded; all other fixed-share heirs participate proportionally.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the surplus goes to bayt al-mal (public treasury), not to the heirs.
This difference has practical consequences.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rationale
Understand the reasoning: spouses inherit by a temporary cause (marriage) that ends at death, so they are not increased. Blood relatives inherit by permanent lineage, so they are worthier of the surplus.
The reasoning is based on the strength or permanence of the inheritance link.
4
Consider the alternative Hanafi reasoning
Another formulation: spouses have only one cause (marriage) while blood relatives have multiple causes (e.g., sonship, brotherhood, womb ties). Hence, spouses cannot receive additional shares.
This emphasizes the plurality of bonds for blood relatives.
5
Recognize the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi law, blood heirs receive extra shares; in Shafi'i, the surplus goes to the state. This affects actual inheritance distribution.
The difference is significant in estate planning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد
والفاضل عن فرض ذوي السهام ـ إذا لم تكن عصبة ـ : مردود عليهم بقدر سهامهم، إلا على الزوجين.
قال رحمه الله : والفاضل عن فرض ذوي السهام إذا لم تكن عصبة مردود عليهم بقدر سهامهم، إلا على الزوجين).
وعند الشافعي: الفاضل لبيت المال.
وإنما لم يرد على الزوجين : لأن فرضهما بالسبب، لا بالنسب، فهو ضعيف؛ لأنهما استحقاه بعد انقطاع السبب الذي يستحقان به، فلا يزادان على فرضهما، بخلاف من يرث بالنسب؛ لأن النسب باق بعد الموت، فقوي حالهم في الاستحقاق، فكانوا أولى بالفاضل.
أو نقول: إن الزوجين يستحقان بسبب واحد، وهو النكاح، فإذا به: لم يكن لهما سبب غير ذلك يستحقان به، وأهل النسب يستحقون بالنسب، وهو البنوة في البنت، والأخوة في الأخت، والباقي بالرحم.
Chapter of Radd. The surplus from the fixed share of the entitled heirs, if there is no agnate, is returned to them according to their shares, except to the spouses. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The surplus from the fixed share of the entitled heirs if there is no agnate is returned to them according to their shares except to the spouses. According to al-Shafi'i: The surplus goes to the public treasury. It is not returned to the spouses because their share is by cause, not by blood relation, so it is weak; indeed they deserve it after the cessation of the cause by which they deserve it, so they are not increased beyond their fixed share, unlike one who inherits by blood relation because blood relation remains after death, so their status in entitlement is strong, so they are more deserving of the surplus. Or we say: The spouses deserve by one cause, which is marriage, so with it they have no other cause by which to deserve, while blood relatives deserve by blood relation, which is sonship for the daughter, brotherhood for the sister, and the rest by womb.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the return of surplus (radd) in inheritance and contrasts it with al-Shafi'i's view. The matn states that after distributing fixed shares, if there is surplus and no residuary heir ('asabah), the surplus is returned to the heirs who received fixed shares, in proportion to their shares. However, spouses (husband or wife) are excluded from this return. Al-Shafi'i holds that the surplus goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The commentary explains the rationale: spouses inherit by cause (sabab), i.e., marriage, which terminates at death, so their share is fixed and not subject to increase; blood relatives inherit by lineage (nasab), which persists after death, so their right is stronger, and they deserve the surplus. An alternative reasoning: spouses have only one cause (marriage) to justify inheritance, while blood relatives have multiple bonds (e.g., sonship, brotherhood, womb ties). Hence, spouses are limited to their fixed share.

Key Terms
الرد (ar-radd): The return of surplus from fixed shares to the heirs (except spouses) in proportion to their entitlements when there is no residuary heir.
الزوجين (az-zawjayn): The husband and wife; both are excluded from receiving radd.
السبب (as-sabab): The legal cause of inheritance, specifically marriage (nikah), which is temporary and ends at death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when there is a surplus after distributing fixed shares and no residuary heir, which heirs are excluded from receiving a return of the surplus?
A) All blood relatives
B) Only the husband
C) Only the wife
D) Both spouses

Key Takeaway
Spouses are excluded from radd because their inheritance is based on a temporary cause, whereas blood relatives inherit by a permanent bond.

Return of Surplus (Radd): The Exception for Spouses
Defines radd with inter-madhhab dispute on spouse exclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of radd, identify the exception for spouses, and explain the Hanafi reasoning distinguishing spouses from lineage heirs.

Core Idea
Surplus returns to all prescribed heirs except spouses because their claim is based on a temporary cause.

In Hanafi inheritance law, when there are Quranic sharers but no agnate (asabah), the surplus after distributing their prescribed shares is returned to them proportionally. However, the husband or wife is excluded from this return. This lesson examines the rule, the Shafi'i opposing view, and the reasoning behind the exception.

Guided Path
1
Define radd and its condition
Identify the situation: after assigning shares to Quranic heirs, if there is no asabah, the surplus is redistributed among those same heirs according to their shares.
Condition: absence of any agnate (asabah).
2
Identify the exception
Note that the husband and wife (the spouses) are excluded from radd. They receive only their prescribed share and no surplus.
Spouses are the only Quranic heirs excluded.
3
Understand the Shafi'i opposing view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the surplus goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), not to the heirs at all.
This is a fundamental difference in the philosophy of surplus distribution.
4
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning
The Hanafi position is based on the distinction between inheritance by cause (sabab) and inheritance by lineage (nasab). Marriage is a temporary cause that ends at death, so spouses cannot claim more than their fixed share. Lineage-based heirs have a permanent connection, making them stronger in entitlement.
Alternative reasoning: spouses have only one cause (marriage), while lineage heirs have multiple ties (daughterhood, sisterhood, etc.).
5
Apply the rule to a sample case
Consider a deceased leaving a daughter (1/2), a mother (1/6), and a husband (1/4). After assigning shares, surplus = 1/12. Under Hanafi, the husband does not participate; surplus is returned to daughter and mother proportionally (daughter gets 2/3 of surplus, mother gets 1/3). Under Shafi'i, surplus goes to bayt al-mal.
Calculate: daughter's share 6/12, mother 2/12, husband 3/12, total 11/12, surplus 1/12.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد
والفاضل عن فرض ذوي السهام ـ إذا لم تكن عصبة ـ : مردود عليهم بقدر سهامهم، إلا على الزوجين.
قال رحمه الله : والفاضل عن فرض ذوي السهام إذا لم تكن عصبة مردود عليهم بقدر سهامهم، إلا على الزوجين).
وعند الشافعي(۱): الفاضل لبيت المال.
وإنما لم يُرَدَّ على الزوجين : لأن فرضهما بالسبب، لا بالنسب، فهو ضعيف؛ لأنهما استحقاه بعد انقطاع السبب الذي يستحقان به، فلا يُزادان على فرضهما، بخلاف من يرث بالنسب؛ لأن النسب باق بعد الموت، فقوي حالهم في الاستحقاق، فكانوا أولى بالفاضل.
أو نقول: إن الزوجين يستحقان بسبب واحد، وهو النكاح، فإذا به: لم يكن لهما سبب غير ذلك يستحقان به، وأهل النسب يستحقون بالنسب، وهو البنوة في البنت، والأخوة في الأخت، والباقي بالرحم.
(1) ينظر مغني المحتاج ٢٠/٣.
Chapter of Return (Radd). And the surplus from the prescribed shares of the entitled heirs — when there is no agnate — is returned to them according to their shares, except to the spouses. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And the surplus from the prescribed shares of the entitled heirs, when there is no agnate, is returned to them according to their shares, except to the spouses.' According to al-Shafi'i, the surplus belongs to the public treasury. It was not returned to the spouses only because their share is by reason (cause) and not by lineage, so it is weak; because they deserve it after the discontinuation of the cause by which they deserve it, so they are not increased beyond their share, unlike those who inherit by lineage because lineage remains after death, so their state of entitlement is strong, making them more entitled to the surplus. Or we say: the spouses deserve by one cause, which is marriage, so they have no other cause by which to deserve, while the people of lineage deserve by lineage, such as daughterhood for the daughter, sisterhood for the sister, and the rest by kinship.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the ruling of radd (return of surplus) in inheritance. The matn states that when there are ashab al-furud (Quranic heirs) but no asabah (agnate), the surplus after distributing their prescribed shares is returned to them proportionally to their shares, except for the husband or wife. The commentator expands by noting the Shafi'i position that the surplus goes to bayt al-mal (public treasury). He then provides the Hanafi reasoning for excluding spouses: their share is based on a temporary cause (marriage), not permanent lineage. Since the cause ends at death, they cannot receive more than their prescribed share. In contrast, lineage-based heirs have a continuous connection, so they are stronger in entitlement. The commentator also gives an alternative explanation: spouses have only one cause (marriage), while lineage heirs have multiple ties (e.g., daughterhood, sisterhood). The Hanafi school's ruling is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): Return of the surplus from prescribed shares to the entitled heirs proportionally when there is no agnate.
عصبة (asabah): An agnate heir who takes the residual after prescribed shares, either by blood or by relation.
ذوو السهام (dhawu al-siham): Heirs entitled to prescribed fractions in the Quran (ashab al-furud).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are spouses excluded from radd (return of surplus)?
A) Because they are not blood relatives and their share is based on a temporary cause (marriage) that ends at death.
B) Because they have already received a large share and do not need more.
C) Because they are always entitled to the surplus if there is no agnate.
D) Because the Quran explicitly excludes them from inheritance.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi faraid, the surplus after prescribed shares is returned to all Quranic heirs except the spouses, based on the distinction between inheritance by cause (sabab) and inheritance by lineage (nasab).

The Killer Does Not Inherit: Rule and Exceptions in Hanafi Fiqh
States the rule of homicide preventing inheritance with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a killer does not inherit from the victim, identify the conditions for deprivation, and list the exceptions where the killer still inherits.

Core Idea
Only deliberate, adult, sane killers lose inheritance; minors, insane, accidental, and legal executions preserve it.

This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayirah presents the foundational rule that a killer does not inherit from his victim, but immediately clarifies that this rule is not absolute. The commentary by Imam al-Haddadi elaborates on the conditions and numerous exceptions, showing that Hanafi law focuses on culpability. Understanding these nuances prevents misapplication of a seemingly straightforward rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
The matn states 'the killer does not inherit from the killed.' The commentary specifies that this applies only to an adult, sane killer. Note that the minor and insane are excluded.
2
List the exceptions for accidental or non-culpable killings
The commentary lists scenarios where death results from the killer's unintentional act (digging a well, placing a stone), legal execution (qisas, stoning), compulsion, or testimony leading to execution. In all these, the killer inherits. Note that the same rule applies if the killer is a son testifying against his father.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Deprivation is based on deliberate, unjustified killing. Absence of intent or legal justification removes the bar to inheritance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَرِثُ القاتل من المقتول .
قوله: (ولا يرثُ القاتل من المقتول، يعني إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً،
ويرث الصبي والمجنون من أبيه إذا قتله.
والبالغ العاقل إذا وقع مورثه في بئر حفرها على الطريق، أو سقط على حجر وضعه في الطريق، أو وُجد الأبُ في دار ابنه قتيلاً، أو قتل مورثه في قصاص أو رجم، أو قتله مكرهاً، أو شهد الابن على أبيه بالزنا: ففي جميع هذه الأشياء لا يُحرم الميراث.
And the killer does not inherit from the killed. His statement: (And the killer does not inherit from the killed), meaning if he is an adult and sane. And the minor and the insane inherit from their father if they kill him. And the adult sane person, if his testator falls into a well that he dug on the road, or falls on a stone that he placed on the road, or the father is found killed in his son's house, or his testator is killed in qisas or stoning, or he kills him under compulsion, or the son gives evidence of adultery against his father: in all these cases, he is not deprived of inheritance.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn that a killer does not inherit from his victim. The sharh clarifies that this applies when the killer is an adult and sane. Exceptions are made for a minor or insane person who kills his father—they do inherit because they lack intent. Additionally, the commentary lists several scenarios where an adult sane killer is not barred from inheritance: if the death results from an accident involving the killer's action (e.g., digging a well, placing a stone), if the death occurs through qisas or stoning (legal execution), if the killing was under compulsion, or if the son testified against the father for adultery (which may lead to execution). In all these cases, the killer is not considered a deliberate murderer for inheritance purposes, so inheritance is allowed.

Key Terms
قاتل (qatil): A person who causes the death of another; in inheritance, the deliberate adult sane killer is deprived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following killers would inherit from the victim according to the Hanafi school?
A) An adult who deliberately shoots his father
B) A minor child who accidentally kills his father
C) A man who kills his brother in a legal execution
D) A woman who poisons her husband out of hatred

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only deliberate, intentional killing by a legally capable person bars inheritance; all other forms of causing death do not affect inheritance rights.

No Inheritance Between Muslim and Disbeliever
Ruling on inheritance across religions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that Muslims and disbelievers do not inherit from each other and explain the milla principle.

Core Idea
Disbelievers inherit as one community; Muslims and disbelievers never inherit from each other.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clarifies a foundational rule in inheritance: religion is a barrier to inheritance. The author states that all forms of disbelief are treated as a single religious community (milla) for inheritance, meaning a Christian can inherit from a Jew, but a Muslim cannot inherit from either.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage makes two related points: (1) all disbelievers are one milla, so they inherit from each other; (2) there is no inheritance between a Muslim and a disbeliever in either direction.
Both are absolute rules with no exceptions in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the milla concept
Milla here means religious community. Hanafis consider all non-Islamic religions as one legal category for inheritance, regardless of internal differences (e.g., Christian, Jew, polytheist).
Other schools may differentiate between People of the Book and polytheists, but Hanafis treat them equally.
3
Apply the rule
If a Christian man dies, his Christian son inherits; if his son converts to Islam before his death, the son does not inherit. Similarly, a Muslim cannot inherit from a disbelieving relative.
This is based on the hadith: 'A Muslim does not inherit from a disbeliever, nor a disbeliever from a Muslim.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

However, the matn states 'فيء' which aligns with Abu Hanifa. The commentary presents both. We note the ikhtilaf.

ملة (millah): Religious community; here it means that all disbelievers are considered one community for inheritance purposes.

Apostate's Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on apostate's post-apostasy property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on property acquired by an apostate during apostasy and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
An apostate's Muslim heirs inherit his pre-apostasy property, but post-apostasy acquisition is disputed: fay' or inheritance.

The previous lesson covered inheritance across religions. Now the commentary turns to the special case of an apostate (murtadd). The matn states that property acquired before apostasy goes to Muslim heirs, but there is an internal Hanafi disagreement on property acquired during apostasy. This lesson unpacks that ikhtilaf and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of the apostate's wealth
The passage distinguishes: (1) property acquired while Muslim, (2) property acquired while apostate. The first goes to Muslim heirs; the second is disputed.
Some later scholars also discuss property from before Islam, but not in this passage.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa says post-apostasy acquisition is fay' (booty for the public treasury). His basis: apostasy invalidates the apostate's right to property; ownership ceases.
This view is mentioned in the matn as 'فيء'.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it goes to Muslim heirs. Two reasons: (a) since disbelieving heirs do not inherit, Muslim heirs step in; (b) ownership does not cease by apostasy, so earnings remain his and thus pass to his heirs.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in later Hanafi fatwa.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the public treasury gets the post-apostasy wealth; under Sahibayn, the Muslim heirs get it.
The Sahibayn's view is preferred because it preserves the rights of Muslim heirs.

The Killer's Inheritance: Rule, Exceptions, and Cross-Madhhab Differences
Commentary on matn ruling with expanded exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six exceptions where a killer inherits and explain the Hanafi distinction between intentional and non-intentional killing.

Core Idea
A killer inherits unless the killing was both intentional and unjust; accidents and legal executions preserve inheritance.

The general rule in Hanafi inheritance law is that a killer does not inherit from his victim. However, this rule is not absolute. The classical Hanafi texts list several exceptions where inheritance is preserved even though the heir caused the death. Understanding these exceptions requires distinguishing between intentional murder, accidental death, and legally sanctioned executions. This lesson unpacks the rule and its exceptions as presented in Al-Jawharah Al-Nayyirah.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its condition
Identify the base ruling: the killer does not inherit from the killed. Note the condition: this applies only if the killer is a sane adult (baligh aqil).
The matn says: 'The killer does not inherit from the killed.' The commentary adds the condition of adulthood and sanity.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'لا يرث القاتل شيئاً' (The killer does not inherit anything) is cited as the primary evidence. It is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
The hadith is general, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan to allow exceptions.
3
List the six exceptions from the commentary
The commentary lists: (1) minor or insane killer, (2) death due to a pit or stone on public road, (3) father found dead in son's house, (4) death by qisas or rajm, (5) killing under compulsion, (6) son testifying against father for adultery leading to death. In these cases, the killer inherits.
The common element: no intentional unjust killing.
4
Identify the cross-madhhab disagreement
The Hanafi school permits inheritance in cases like death by qisas (retaliation). Some Shafi'i scholars bar inheritance even in such cases, considering any direct causation of death as a bar.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether the heir inherits after a legal execution.
5
Evaluate the underlying principle
The Hanafi position rests on the principle that only intentional unjust killing (qatl al-'udwan) nullifies inheritance. Accidental, legally compelled, or justified killings do not deprive the heir.
This is an application of istihsan, preferring a lenient ruling over the literal generality of the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد
ولا يَرِثُ القاتل من المقتول .
والكفر كلَّه مِلَّةٌ واحدة، يتوارث به أهله . رُكلُّه.
ولا يَرِثُ المسلم من الكافر، ولا الكافر من المسلم .
٤٤٩
ومال المرتد : لورثته من المسلمين وما اكتسبه في حال ردته : فيء.
قوله: (ولا يرثُ القاتل من المقتول، يعني إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً،
ويرث الصبي والمجنون من أبيه إذا قتله.
والبالغ العاقل إذا وقع مورثه في بئر حفرها على الطريق، أو سقط على حجر وضعه في الطريق، أو وُجد الأبُ في دار ابنه قتيلاً، أو قتل مورثه في قصاص أو رجم، أو قتله مكرهاً، أو شهد الابن على أبيه بالزنا: ففي جميع هذه الأشياء لا يُحرم الميراث.
Chapter of Return (Inheritance). The killer does not inherit from the killed. And disbelief all together is one religion; its people inherit from each other. And the Muslim does not inherit from the disbeliever, nor the disbeliever from the Muslim. 449. And the property of the apostate goes to his Muslim heirs, and what he earned during his apostasy is fay' (booty). He said: (The killer does not inherit from the killed), meaning if he is an adult and sane. But a minor and an insane person inherit from their father if they kill him. And the adult sane person if his legator falls into a well he dug on the road, or falls on a stone he placed on the road, or the father is found dead in his son's house, or he kills his legator in retaliation or stoning, or he kills him under compulsion, or the son testifies against his father for adultery: in all these cases he is not deprived of inheritance.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the matn of Al-Quduri, expanded by the commentary of Al-Jawharah Al-Nayyirah. The base ruling is that a killer does not inherit from his victim, provided the killer is a sane adult. The commentary then lists exceptions where inheritance is not prevented even if the heir causes death: if the killer is a minor or insane, death from a pit or stone left on a public road, accidental death in the heir's house, death by legal execution (qisas or rajm), killing under compulsion, or testifying to adultery against the father. In these cases, the law presumes no deliberate murder intent, so inheritance is preserved. This is the Hanafi position based on the principle that intentional murder (without right) bars inheritance, but not accidental or legally sanctioned acts.

Key Terms
قاتل (qātil): The person who causes the death of another, including intentional, accidental, or legally sanctioned killing.
قصاص (qisās): Retaliation, a legal punishment where the victim's heir may execute the murderer.
رجم (rajm): Stoning, a legal punishment for adultery under Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the death resulted from retaliation (qisas)
C) When the killer is a minor
D) When the killer is a spouse

Key Takeaway
A killer is only barred from inheritance if the killing is intentional and unjust; otherwise, inheritance is preserved.

Simultaneous Death and Inheritance: The Hanafi Presumption
States ruling when order of death unknown in mass casualty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance when the order of death is unknown in a mass casualty and explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind it.

Core Idea
When deaths occur together and order is unknown, no one inherits from another—all property goes to living heirs.

In inheritance law, the order of deaths can determine who inherits. This lesson examines the Hanafi rule for mass casualty situations where the sequence cannot be known. The passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar establishes a clear presumption that prevents mutual inheritance among the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The text presents a specific fact pattern: a group drowns or a wall collapses on them, and it is unknown who died first.
The scenario must involve a single event causing multiple deaths with undetermined timing.
2
State the Ruling
The property of each deceased goes to his living heirs, and no one among the dead inherits from another.
The ruling denies inheritance between the deceased; it does not affect inheritance by living relatives.
3
Understand the Legal Presumption
The commentator explains the reason: 'because they are judged to have died together' (yuhkamu bimawtihim ma'an). This is a legal fiction that treats all deaths as simultaneous.
The presumption is applied when the actual sequence cannot be determined; it is not a factual finding.
4
Identify the Evidence (Talil)
The evidence is the principle of simultaneous death, derived from the impossibility of ascertaining priority. The text gives the reason directly.
No external proof (Qur'an, hadith) is cited here; the reasoning is based on legal logic and the need for certainty.
5
Apply the Principle
In any mass casualty with unknown sequence, the same rule applies (e.g., airplane crash, earthquake). No need to investigate timing; the presumption governs.
The ruling is consistent across all similar scenarios; it is a general rule in Hanafi faraid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد. وإذا غرق جماعة أو سقط عليهم حائط فلم يعلم من مات منهم أولاً فمال كل واحد منهم للأحياء من ورثته ولا يرث بعضهم من بعض لأنه يحكم بموتهم معاً.
Chapter on Return. When a group drowns or a wall falls upon them and it is not known who died first, the property of each one belongs to the living among his heirs, and none of them inherits from the other because they are judged to have died together.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on Inheritance by Return (Radd). The matn states: When a group of people drown in the same incident, or a wall collapses on them, and the order of their deaths cannot be determined, then the estate of each deceased person goes entirely to his living heirs. No one among the deceased inherits from another because they are legally deemed to have died at the same moment. This is based on the principle that when the sequence of death is unknown, we treat it as simultaneous, thus preventing mutual inheritance. The commentator explains the reasoning: 'because they are judged to have died together.' This ruling applies to any situation where multiple deaths occur in a single event and the timing cannot be established.

Key Terms
يحكم بموتهم معاً (yuhkamu bimawtihim ma'an): Legal presumption that deaths occurred at the same moment when the sequence is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Each deceased's property goes to their living heirs, and none inherits from another
C) The property is divided equally among all estates
D) The state takes possession of all property

Key Takeaway
When the order of death in a mass casualty is unknown, Hanafi law presumes simultaneous death, preventing inheritance among the deceased.

Inheritance from an Apostate According to Abu Hanifa
States Abu Hanifa's ruling and condition for inheriting from an apostate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on the apostate's estate, explain the qiyas analogy, and apply the condition for inheritance from an apostate.

Core Idea
Apostate's property is fay'; heir's status is fixed at apostasy, not death.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling on the estate of an apostate. Abu Hanifa (RA) argues that the apostate's property is treated as fay' (booty) due to his legal status as mubah al-dam (permissible to kill). Furthermore, the inheritance rights of his heirs are determined at the moment of apostasy, not at his death. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the qiyas analogy to a harbi and the fixed condition of the heir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of an apostate
Recognize that Abu Hanifa considers the apostate as mubah al-dam (his blood is permissible), which triggers the treatment of his property.
This status is key to the analogy.
2
Understand the qiyas analogy
Compare the apostate to a harbi (enemy combatant). Both are mubah al-dam, so the apostate's property is treated like a harbi's property: it becomes fay'.
The illa (effective cause) is being mubah al-dam.
3
Note the rule for inheritance
The heir's status is considered on the day of apostasy, not at death or any later event.
4
Apply the rule to different cases
If the heir was free and Muslim on the day of apostasy, he inherits. If he was a slave or disbeliever, he does not inherit, even if he later becomes free or converts.
The condition is fixed at apostasy; subsequent changes do not affect it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأبي حنيفة: أن المرتد مباح الدم، فوجب أن يكون ما في يده في تلك الحالة فيئاً، كمال الحربي. ثم على قول أبي حنيفة: وارث المرتد يُعتبر حاله يوم الردة، فإن كان حراً مسلماً يوم ردته ورثه، وإن كان عبداً أو كافراً يوم الردة: لم يرثه، وإن أعتق أو أسلم قبل أن يموت، أو يُقتل، أو يُحكم بلحاقه: لم يرثه.
According to Abu Hanifa: the apostate is of permissible blood [i.e., can be killed], so it is necessary that what is in his hand in that state becomes fay' (spoils), like the property of a warring disbeliever. Then according to Abu Hanifa's statement: the condition of the apostate's heir is considered on the day of apostasy; if he was free and Muslim on the day of his apostasy, he inherits from him; but if he was a slave or disbeliever on the day of apostasy, he does not inherit from him; and if he becomes free or accepts Islam before the apostate dies, is killed, or is adjudged to have joined the enemy, he does not inherit.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on the estate of an apostate. Abu Hanifa holds that an apostate is legally 'mubah al-dam' (his blood is permissible) because he has abandoned Islam. Consequently, his property in that state is treated as fay' (booty), analogous to the wealth of a harbi (enemy combatant). This means the apostate's estate does not pass to his Muslim heirs by normal inheritance but becomes part of the public treasury. However, Abu Hanifa details that the qualification of the heir is assessed on the day of apostasy, not at death. If the heir was free and Muslim at the time the deceased apostatized, he inherits; but if the heir was a slave or disbeliever at that moment, he does not inherit, even if he later becomes free or converts. The condition is fixed at apostasy.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): One who has apostatized from Islam.
مباح الدم (mubah al-dam): Legally permissible to kill without liability.
فيء (fay'): Spoils or property taken from enemies without war; here the apostate's estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, at what time is the condition of the heir considered for inheriting from an apostate?
A) At the time of the apostate's death
B) At the time of the heir's birth
C) On the day of apostasy
D) At the time of the apostate's capture

Key Takeaway
The apostate's estate is fay', and the heir's qualification is fixed on the day of apostasy.

Magian Inheritance Through Multiple Relationships
Explains Magian inheritance with multiple relations and invalid marriages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for Magian inheritance when a Magian has multiple kinship ties, distinguishing valid marriages from invalid ones and calculating shares accordingly.

Core Idea
A Magian inherits through each valid cause, but invalid marriages never create inheritance — only valid lineage counts.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains how Magians inherit when they have multiple familial relationships, such as being both mother and wife. The key principle is that only relationships that would be valid in Islam create inheritance rights, even if the Magian religion considers them valid. Understanding these rules helps us apply Islamic inheritance law to non-Muslim cases with precision.

Guided Path
1
State the General Principle
Identify the core ruling: A Magian inherits through every valid relationship (lineage or valid marriage) that would cause inheritance in Islam. Invalid marriages do not create inheritance rights.
The proof is qiyas: since invalid marriages do not cause inheritance among Muslims, they also do not cause inheritance among Magians.
2
Analyze the First Example
Work through the example: A Magian marries his mother (invalid in Islam), has a daughter. He dies leaving mother/wife and daughter/sister. Determine shares: mother gets 1/6 by motherhood (not blocked by daughter), daughter gets 1/2, residue to asaba. Note that mother does not inherit as wife (invalid marriage) nor as sister (blocked by daughter).
The mother is both wife and sister, but only motherhood gives a share.
3
Analyze the Second Example
A Magian marries his daughter, has two daughters. He dies, then one daughter dies leaving a mother (who is also her sister) and a full sister. Calculate: mother gets 1/6 by motherhood; sister through both parents gets 1/2; mother also gets another 1/6 by sisterhood (since the mother is also a sister to the deceased), because her sisterhood is treated as if from another person, causing the mother to be blocked from 1/3 to 1/6.
This illustrates inheritance through multiple causes when one cause does not block the other.
4
Apply the Test for Valid Marriage
Learn the test: If the couple became Muslim, would their marriage be upheld? If yes, it is valid for inheritance; if no, it is invalid. This test determines whether a Magian marriage confers inheritance rights.
Only marriages valid in Islam count, even if the Magian religion permits them.
5
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Other schools may have different views on the validity of Magian marriages for inheritance. The Hanafi position relies strictly on Islamic validity, which can lead to different distributions than other schools.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi ruling is mufta bihi in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد. وإذا اجتمع في المجوسي قرابتان لو تفرقتا في شخصين ورث أحدهما من الآخر ورث بهما. وإذا اجتمعا في شخص ورث بهما جميعاً: تفسيره: مجوسي تزوج أمه فولدت له بنتاً ثم مات عن أم هي زوجته وعن بنت هي أخته لأمه: فلا ترث الأم بالزوجية ولا ابنته بالأختية لأن الأخت للأم لا ترث مع الابنة ولكن للأم السدس باعتبار الأمومية وللابنة النصف والباقي للعصبة. ولا يرث المجوسي بالأنكحة الفاسدة التي يستحلونها في دينهم. مجوسي تزوج بنته فولدت له ابنتين فمات المجوسي ثم ماتت إحدى الابنتين: فإنها ماتت عن أم هي أخت لأب وعن أخت لأب وأم: فللأم السدس بالأمومية وللأخت للأب والأم النصف وللأم السدس بالأختية للأب؛ لأنا لما اعتبرنا الأختية للأب التي وجدت في الأم لاستحقاق السدس: بها صار ذلك كالموجود في شخص آخر، كأنها تركت الأختين، وهما يحجبان الأم من الثلث إلى السدس. كذا في «المستصفى». قوله: (ولا يرث المجوسي بالأنكحة الفاسدة التي يستحلونها في دينهم)؛ لأن النكاح الفاسد لا يوجب التوارث بين المسلمين فلا يوجبه بين المجوس بخلاف الأنساب. والأصل أن المجوس يرثون بالزوجية إذا كان النكاح بينهما جائزاً، فإن لم يكن بينهما جائزاً فإنهما لا يتوارثان بالزوجية. ومعرفة الجائز من الفاسد: أن كل نكاح لو أسلما يُتركان عليه: فذلك نكاح جائز وما لا يُتركان عليه فهو فاسد. وما كان يُدلي بسببين وأحدهما لا يحجب الآخر فإنه يرث بالسببين وإن كان أحدهما يحجب الآخر فإنه يرث بالحاجب ولا يرث بالمحجوب. بيانه: مجوسي ترك زوجةً هي أمه وهي أخته لأبيه، كما إذا تزوج ابنته فولدت منه ولداً ثم تزوج هذا أمه، هذا أمه، وهي أخته لأبيه: فإن هذا
Chapter on Return. When a Magian has two relationships that, if they were in two separate persons, one would inherit from the other, he inherits through both. And when both combine in one person, he inherits through both completely. Explanation: A Magian married his mother and she gave birth to a daughter; then he dies leaving a mother who is his wife and a daughter who is his sister through his mother: the mother does not inherit through the marital relationship, nor does the daughter through the sisterhood, because the sister through mother does not inherit with the daughter; but the mother gets one-sixth by motherhood, the daughter gets half, and the rest goes to the asaba. The Magian does not inherit through invalid marriages that they consider lawful in their religion. A Magian married his daughter and she gave birth to two daughters; then the Magian died, then one of the two daughters died: she died leaving a mother who is a sister through father, and a sister through father and mother: for the mother is one-sixth by motherhood, for the sister through father and mother is half, and for the mother is one-sixth through sisterhood to father; because we considered the sisterhood to the father that existed in the mother for entitlement to one-sixth: thereby it became as if it existed in another person, as if she left two sisters, and they block the mother from one-third to one-sixth. This is in 'al-Mustasfa'. He said: (The Magian does not inherit through invalid marriages that they consider lawful in their religion) because an invalid marriage does not cause inheritance between Muslims, so it does not cause it between Magians, unlike lineages. The principle is that Magians inherit through marriage if the marriage between them is valid; if it is not valid, then they do not inherit through marriage. The way to know valid from invalid is: every marriage that, if they became Muslim, would be allowed to remain: that is a valid marriage; and what they would not be allowed to remain upon is invalid. And whoever claims through two causes and one does not block the other: he inherits through both causes; but if one blocks the other, then he inherits through the blocker and does not inherit through the blocked. Explanation: A Magian leaves a wife who is his mother and his sister through his father, such as when he married his daughter and she gave birth to a son, then this one married his mother... [text breaks off]

Commentary
This passage addresses the inheritance rules for Magians (Zoroastrians) under Islamic law. It begins with the matn stating that when a Magian has two relationships that would each entitle inheritance separately, he inherits through both. The commentator provides examples: if a Magian marries his mother (a marriage considered valid in his religion but invalid in Islam), he leaves a mother/wife and a daughter/sister. The mother does not inherit by marriage (because it's invalid) nor by sisterhood (because sister through mother is blocked by the daughter), but she inherits one-sixth by motherhood. The daughter inherits half as a daughter, and the remainder goes to agnates. This illustrates that inheritance by lineage is considered, not invalid marriage. Another example: a Magian marries his daughter, producing two daughters. When one daughter dies, she leaves a mother (who is also a sister through father) and a sister (through both parents). The mother gets one-sixth by motherhood and another one-sixth by sisterhood through father, because the sisterhood is considered as if another person, causing the mother to be treated as having two sisters blocking her from one-third to one-sixth. The commentator then explains that Magians do not inherit through invalid marriages, using the test: if the couple became Muslim, would their marriage be upheld? If yes, it's valid for inheritance; if no, it's invalid. A further principle: if someone connects through two causes, and one does not block the other, he inherits through both. But if one blocks the other, he inherits only through the blocking cause. The example of a Magian who marries his daughter and then his daughter's son marries his mother (the first Magian's mother) is given but incomplete.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majusi): A follower of the Zoroastrian religion; a Magian.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage that is invalid in Islamic law but may be considered valid in the Magian religion.
حجب (hajb): Blocking or reducing inheritance share due to the presence of a closer heir.

Magian Inheritance: Invalid Marriages and Multiple Causes
Ruling with proof on Magian inheritance and multiple causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that invalid marriages do not create inheritance rights for Magians and calculate shares when multiple causes exist.

Core Idea
Invalid marriages among Magians yield no inheritance; blood ties and valid marriages do, with careful cause analysis.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira tackles a unique corner of Hanafi faraid: inheritance among Magians (Zoroastrians). The key challenge is that Magians often practice marriages forbidden in Islam (e.g., incest). The Hanafi school evaluates such marriages by Islamic standards: only valid Islamic marriages create inheritance rights. Moreover, when a person claims inheritance through multiple relationships, we must see if one cause excludes another. By working through this passage, you will learn how to distinguish valid from invalid marriages and apply the principle of multiple causes to compute shares.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the legal rule: Magians do not inherit through marriages that are invalid (fasid) according to Islamic law. Note that this is a negative ruling—invalid marriage is not a cause of inheritance.
The ruling is based on qiyas: if invalid marriage does not create inheritance among Muslims, it does not among Magians either.
2
Understand the criterion for valid vs. invalid marriage
The passage provides a test: if both parties converted to Islam and would be allowed to remain in that marriage, it is valid; otherwise it is invalid. This test uses Islamic marriage validity as the standard.
This criterion applies only to Magians, not to Muslims.
3
Learn the principle of multiple causes
When a person claims inheritance through two different causes (e.g., being both a mother and a sister), if one cause does not exclude (hajb) the other, he inherits through both. If one excludes, he inherits only through the excluding cause.
This is about the causes themselves, not the persons. In standard faraid, a son would exclude a brother, but here the same person is both; the text allows inheritance through both because the causes are independent.
4
Work through the first example
A Magian leaves a wife who is also his mother and paternal sister. The marriage is invalid, so no spousal inheritance. He inherits as a son (1/3) and as a brother (1/2) because one cause does not exclude the other. Total 5/6, remainder returned to him.
The shares (1/3 and 1/2) are not standard Islamic faraid but are used here as a specific ruling for Magians.
5
Work through the second example
A Magian leaves a wife who is also his daughter and maternal sister. She inherits as a daughter (1/2) but not as a maternal sister because a maternal sister is excluded by a direct descendant (the daughter).
This shows the application of hajb: the closer cause (daughter) excludes the more distant one (maternal sister).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد
ولا يرث المجوسي بالأنكحة الفاسدة التي يستحلونها في دينهم.
قوله: (ولا يرث المجوسي بالأنكحة الفاسدة التي يستحلونها في دينهم)؛ لأن النكاح الفاسد : لا يوجب التوارث بين المسلمين، فلا يوجبه بين المجوس، بخلاف الأنساب.
والأصل أن المجوس يرثون بالزوجية إذا كان النكاح بينهما جائزاً، فإن لم يكن بينهما جائزاً: فإنهما لا يتوارثان بالزوجية.
ومعرفة الجائز من الفاسد: أن كل نكاح لو أسلما يُتركان عليه: فذلك نكاح جائز، وما لا يُتركان عليه : فهو فاسد.
وما كان يُدلي بسببين وأحدهما لا يحجب الآخر: فإنه يرث بالسببين، وإن كان أحدهما يحجب الآخر فإنه يرث بالحاجب، ولا يرث بالمحجوب.
بيانه: مجوسي ترك زوجةً هي أمه وهي أخته لأبيه، كما إذا تزوج ابنته، فولدت منه ولداً، ثم تزوج هذا أمه، هذا أمه، وهي أخته لأبيه: فإن هذا النكاح فاسد، لا يرث بالزوجية، ويرث ثلث المال؛ لأنها أمه، ويرث أيضاً نصف المال؛ لأنها أخته لأبيه، فيرث بالسببين جميعاً؛ لأن أحدهما لا يحجب الآخر، والباقي ردُّ عليهما بالسببين جميعاً إن لم تكن عصبة. ولو ترك امرأة وهي ابنته وهي أخته لأمه، كما إذا تزوج أمه، مات: فلها النصف بكونها بنتاً، ثم لا ترث بكونها أختاً لأم؛ لأن الأخت للأم لا ترث مع ولد الصلب.
Chapter of Return. The Magian does not inherit through invalid marriages that they consider permissible in their religion. His statement: (The Magian does not inherit through invalid marriages that they consider permissible in their religion) because an invalid marriage does not necessitate inheritance between Muslims, so it does not necessitate it between Magians, contrary to blood relations. The principle is that Magians inherit through matrimony if the marriage between them is valid. If it is not valid between them, then they do not inherit through matrimony. The knowledge of valid from invalid: every marriage that if they both became Muslim they would be left upon it is a valid marriage, and whatever they would not be left upon is invalid. And whoever claims through two causes and one does not exclude the other inherits through both causes; if one excludes the other, he inherits through the excluding one and does not inherit through the excluded one. Its clarification: A Magian leaves a wife who is his mother and his paternal sister, as if he married his daughter, she gave birth to a daughter [from him], then this son married his mother, who is his maternal sister and his paternal sister: this marriage is invalid; he does not inherit through matrimony, and he inherits one-third of the property because she is his mother, and he also inherits half of the property because she is his paternal sister; he inherits through both causes because one does not exclude the other, and the remainder is returned to them through both causes if there is no agnate. And if he leaves a woman who is his daughter and his maternal sister, as if he married his mother and she gave birth to a daughter: she gets half as a daughter, then she does not inherit as a maternal sister because the maternal sister does not inherit with a direct descendant.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding inheritance for Magians. It opens by stating the general rule: Magians do not inherit through marriages that are invalid according to Islamic law, even if those marriages are considered valid in their own religion. The reasoning is that an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) does not create inheritance rights among Muslims, so by analogy it also does not create inheritance rights among Magians, unlike blood relations which do create rights. The text then establishes a criterion for distinguishing valid from invalid marriages: if both parties were to become Muslim and would be allowed to remain in that marriage, it is valid; otherwise, it is invalid. Next, the principle of multiple causes is introduced: when a person claims inheritance through two different relationships (e.g., being a mother and a sister), and one does not exclude the other, he inherits through both; if one excludes the other, he inherits only through the excluding one. This is illustrated with two examples: a Magian who leaves a wife who is also his mother and paternal sister—he inherits from her as a mother (1/3) and as a paternal sister (1/2), totaling 5/6, with the remainder returned to him proportionally if no agnate heir. In the second example, the woman is both daughter and maternal sister; she inherits only as a daughter (1/2) because a maternal sister is excluded by a direct descendant. The passage underscores the Hanafi emphasis on proper causal relationships in inheritance.

Key Terms
المجوسي (al-majusi): A Zoroastrian; in this context, a non-Muslim whose marriages are judged by Islamic criteria for inheritance.
النكاح الفاسد (al-nikah al-fasid): A marriage that is invalid due to a legal impediment (e.g., incest) and does not create inheritance rights.
الحاجب (al-hajib): The cause that excludes another; the stronger relationship that prevents inheritance through a weaker one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He inherits only as a mother (1/3) because the sister relationship is excluded.
B) He inherits only as a paternal sister (1/2) because that is the stronger cause.
C) He inherits as both mother (1/3) and paternal sister (1/2) because neither cause excludes the other.
D) He does not inherit because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Invalid marriages do not create inheritance rights among Magians; when multiple causes coexist, check if one excludes the other to determine the share.

Asabah of the Child of Zina and Li'an
States the agnatic heir for children of zina and li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the agnatic heir for children of zina/li'an and calculate the distribution of their estate among the mother, maternal siblings, and mother's agnates.

Core Idea
The child of zina or li'an has no father; his 'asabah is his mother's master (mawla).

In Hanafi inheritance law, children born from zina (adultery) or li'an (mutual imprecation) have no recognized paternal lineage. This lesson clarifies who inherits from them and in what order. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying faraid in cases where paternity is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deceased and the special status
Note that the deceased is a child of zina or li'an, meaning he has no legal father. His lineage is only through the mother.
This is a key principle: no paternal 'asabah.
2
List the heirs in order
The mother is the primary heir. Then maternal siblings (children of the mother from other marriages) share. Finally, the mother's 'asabah (her patron/mawla or his agnates) take the residue.
Hanafi order: mother, maternal siblings, mother's agnates.
3
Apply the fractional shares
Mother gets her prescribed share (e.g., 1/6 if there are siblings). Maternal siblings get their shares (one maternal brother gets 1/6, two or more get 1/3). The remainder goes to mother's agnates.
Shares are based on general faraid rules for maternal relations.
4
Handle the absence of agnates
If the mother has no agnates (e.g., she is a free woman with no patrons), the remainder is returned (radd) to the mother and maternal siblings proportionally.
Radd is a Hanafi principle to prevent the estate from being forfeited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وعصبة ولد الزنا، وولد الملاعنة : مولى أُمِّهما)؛ لأن ولد الزنا لما لم يكن له أب: تعلق ذلك بأمه. وكذا ولد الملاعنة من الأمهات، فإذا مات ذلك الولد: يكون ميراثه لأمه، وأولاد أمه، الذكر والأنثى فيه سواء. فإذا ترك أخاً، أو إخوةً من أم فللواحد السدس، وللاثنين فصاعداً الثلث، وما بقي بعد ميراث الأم وأولادها يكون لعصبة الأم، الأقرب فالأقرب. فإن كانت مولاة لقوم : كان الباقي لموالي أمه، أو لعصبة موالي أُمه. وإن لم تكن عصبة : فالباقي رد على الأم وأولادها.
His statement: (And the agnatic heir of the child of zina and the child of li'an is the master of his mother) because the child of zina, having no father, is attached to his mother. Similarly, the child of li'an from the mother's side. So when that child dies, his inheritance goes to his mother and his mother's children, male and female being equal therein. If he leaves a brother or brothers from the mother, then for one (brother) is a sixth, and for two or more a third. What remains after the inheritance of the mother and her children goes to the agnates of the mother, the nearest then the nearest. If she is a freedwoman of a clan, the remainder goes to the patrons of her mother, or to the agnates of the patrons of her mother. If there is no agnate, the remainder is returned to the mother and her children.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school detailing the inheritance of children born from zina (adultery) and li'an (mutual imprecation). The key point is that such children have no recognized paternal lineage, so their 'asabah (agnatic heirs) are the masters (mawla) of their mother. Since there is no father, the estate is first distributed to the mother and her children (i.e., the deceased's maternal siblings), with the remainder going to the mother's 'asabah—her patrons or their agnates. If the mother herself is a freed slave, the residue goes to her manumitters. If no agnates exist, the surplus is returned (radd) to the mother and her children. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that lineage determines inheritance, and in the absence of paternal lineage, maternal relations and manumission ties take precedence.

Key Terms
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): A child born from adultery, who has no recognized father in Islamic law.
ولد الملاعنة (walad al-mula'anah): A child from a li'an case, where the husband denies paternity and the marriage is dissolved; the child has no father.
مولى (mawla): In this context, the patron (manumitter) of the mother, who becomes the 'asabah for the child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The mother's agnates or her patrons
C) The state treasury
D) No one; the remainder is lost

Key Takeaway
For children of zina or li'an, the 'asabah is the mother's master (mawla), and in his absence, the estate is returned (radd) to the mother and maternal relatives.

Inheritance Suspension for a Pregnant Widow: Abu Hanifa's View
Ruling on waiting for pregnancy to distribute estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on estate distribution when the deceased leaves a pregnant wife and explain the practical difference from the Sahibayn's likely view.

Core Idea
A fetus is a potential heir; Abu Hanifa halts all inheritance until live birth.

In Hanafi inheritance law, the unborn child (al-haml) presents a unique dilemma: its status as an heir is contingent on live birth. Therefore, its share cannot be allocated until that certainty is established. Abu Hanifa held that the entire estate must be withheld until the widow delivers. This ruling prioritises the rights of the potential heir, but it also freezes the inheritance for all other heirs, raising questions of practicality. The phrase 'fi qawl Abi Hanifa' indicates that his two senior students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, may have favoured a different approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and Abu Hanifa's exact ruling
The text says: whoever dies leaving a fetus, his property is withheld until his wife gives birth. This is Abu Hanifa's saying. Note the act: the estate is suspended.
2
Recognise the indicator of ikhtilaf
The phrase 'في قول أبي حنيفة' signals that this is specifically Abu Hanifa's position, not necessarily the unanimous Hanafi view. It implies a disagreement.
3
Infer the opposing position
From the omission of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's view, we deduce they likely held that immediate distribution with a reserved portion is permissible. The text does not elaborate, but the thamarah al-khilaf is clear.
4
Understand the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, everyone waits; under the Sahibayn, most heirs receive their shares earlier. The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice may prefer the Sahibayn's view for ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن مات وتَرَكَ حَمْلاً: وقف ماله حتى تضع امرأته، في قول أبي حنيفة).
His statement: (And whoever dies leaving a fetus: his property is withheld until his wife gives birth, according to the saying of Abu Hanifa).
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the estate of a deceased man whose wife is pregnant. Abu Hanifa's position is that distribution must be delayed until after childbirth to determine the child's share. The implication is that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani likely held a different view—perhaps that the estate can be distributed immediately with a portion reserved for the potential heir, or that the pregnancy does not require a full halt. The text states only Abu Hanifa's view, indicating reliance on his authority in this matter. This ruling ensures that the unborn child's inheritance rights are protected until its status as a living heir is confirmed.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): A fetus; an unborn child that may be an heir if born alive.
في قول أبي حنيفة (fi qawl Abi Hanifa): An indicator that this is Abu Hanifa's personal view, suggesting possible disagreement from his students.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Distribute immediately, ignoring the fetus.
B) Withhold the entire estate until the wife gives birth.
C) Distribute all except a portion reserved for the fetus.
D) Give the wife full control over the estate.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling ensures the unborn child's inheritance is protected by freezing the entire estate until live birth.

intermediate
8 min
The 'Asaba of Children Born from Zina and Li'an
Ruling on 'asaba and shares for children of zina and li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'asaba of a child born from zina or li'an, apply the inheritance shares for the mother and uterine siblings, and determine the residue distribution including radd.

Core Idea
A child from zina or li'an has no paternal lineage; inheritance flows exclusively through the mother and her relatives.

This lesson clarifies a unique inheritance scenario in Hanafi fiqh: children born outside a valid marriage (zina) or from li'an have no recognized father. Consequently, their estate is distributed only through the maternal side. The passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira states that the 'asaba (residuary heir) of such a child is the mawla of the mother, or if she is a free woman, her agnatic relatives. We will examine the shares of the mother and uterine siblings, and the application of radd (return) when no 'asaba exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'asaba
The matn states: 'وعصبة ولد الزنا، وولد الملاعنة : مولى أُمِّهما'. So the residuary heir is the mother's mawla (former master) or, by extension, her agnatic relatives. This is because the child has no father.
If the mother is a free woman, her 'asaba are her agnates (e.g., her brothers).
2
Understand the shares of the mother and uterine siblings
The passage explains that if the child dies leaving a mother and uterine siblings (children of the same mother), the mother takes her Quranic share (1/3 if no siblings, 1/6 if there are siblings). A single uterine brother gets 1/6; two or more get 1/3 collectively. These are fard shares.
Uterine siblings are those sharing only the same mother.
3
Distribute the residue to the 'asaba
After the mother and uterine siblings take their shares, the remainder goes to the mother's 'asaba (her agnates). If no such 'asaba exists, the residue is returned (radd) to the mother and her children (the uterine siblings) in proportion to their shares.
Radd applies only when there is no 'asaba.
4
Analyze the invalid marriage example
The passage includes an example of a man who married his own mother; the resulting daughter inherits as a daughter (1/2) but not as a sister from the mother (uterine sister) because a direct child excludes uterine siblings. This illustrates the exclusion rule: al-ukht li-umm la t'arith ma'a walad al-sulbi.
This example reinforces that uterine siblings are excluded by a direct descendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرد
حنيفة .
وعصبة ولد الزنا، وولد الملاعنة : مولى أُمِّهما .
النكاح فاسد، لا يرث بالزوجية، ويرث ثلث المال؛ لأنها أمه، ويرث أيضاً نصف المال؛ لأنها أخته لأبيه، فيرث بالسببين جميعاً؛ لأن أحدهما لا يحجب الآخر ، والباقي ردُّ عليهما بالسببين جميعاً إن لم تكن عصبة. ولو ترك امرأة وهي ابنته وهي أخته لأمه، كما إذا تزوج أمه ، مات : فلها النصف بكونها بنتاً،
ثم

أُمِّهما ) ؛ لأن ولد الزنا لما لم يكن له أب: تعلق ذلك بأمه.
وكذا ولد الملاعنة من الأمهات، فإذا مات ذلك الولد: يكون ميراثه

فالأقرب.
فإن كانت مولاة لقوم : كان الباقي لموالي أمه، أو لعصبة موالي أُمه.
وإن لم تكن عصبة : فالباقي رد على الأم وأولادها.
Chapter of Return. Hanifa. And the 'asaba of the child of zina and the child of li'an is the mawla of his mother. The marriage is invalid, he does not inherit by marriage, and he inherits one-third of the wealth because she is his mother, and he also inherits half of the wealth because she is his sister from his father, so he inherits by both causes because one does not exclude the other, and the remainder is returned to them by both causes if there is no 'asaba. And if he leaves a woman who is his daughter and his sister from his mother, as if he married his mother, he died: she gets half as a daughter, then she gave birth to a daughter for him, so this one is his daughter and his sister from his mother, and she does not inherit as a sister from the mother because the sister from the mother does not inherit with a child of the loins. His saying: (And the 'asaba of the child of zina and the child of li'an from the mothers is the mawla of his mother) because the child of zina has no father, so that is attached to his mother. Likewise the child of li'an from the mothers. When that child dies, his inheritance goes to his mother and the children of his mother, male and female are equal in it. If he leaves a brother or brothers from the mother, for one is one-sixth, for two or more is one-third, and what remains after the inheritance of the mother and her children is for the 'asaba of the mother, the nearest then the nearest. If she is a mawla of a people, the remainder goes to the mawali of her mother, or to the 'asaba of the mawali of her mother. And if there is no 'asaba, the remainder is returned to the mother and her children.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the inheritance of children born from zina (adultery) and li'an (mutual imprecation). The commentator begins by quoting the matn: the 'asaba (residuary heir) of such a child is the mawla (master/freed slave) of his mother. Since the child has no recognized paternal lineage, inheritance is limited to the maternal side. The passage then gives an example of an invalid marriage where a man marries his own mother; the resulting daughter inherits as a daughter (half) but not as a sister from the mother because the uterine sister is excluded by a direct child. The explanation emphasizes that children of zina and li'an inherit through their mother and her children, with shares specified: one-sixth for a single uterine brother, one-third for two or more. Any residue after the mother and her children goes to the mother's 'asaba (her agnatic relatives), or if no 'asaba, the remainder is returned (radd) to the mother and her children.

Key Terms
عصبة ('asaba): In this context, the agnatic relatives (residuary heirs) who inherit the remainder after the Quranic shares are distributed.
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): A child born from an illicit relationship (zina), who has no legal paternal lineage.
ولد الملاعنة (walad al-mula'anah): A child born from li'an (mutual imprecation), where the husband denies paternity and the marriage is dissolved; the child is attributed to the mother only.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the share of two uterine siblings when a child of zina dies?
A) One-sixth collectively
B) One-third collectively
C) One-half collectively
D) They do not inherit

Key Takeaway
The 'asaba of a child from zina/li'an is the mother's mawla or agnates; inheritance is confined to the maternal side, with specific shares for the mother and uterine siblings, and radd applies when no 'asaba exists.
