D) Analogy with residuary heirs

Key Takeaway
Dhawi al-arham inherit only when no asabah or fixed-share heirs exist; their order of inheritance is by closeness, similar to 'asabah, and the Quran 33:6 supports their priority over the public treasury.

Inheritance of Dhawu al-Arham and Order of Precedence
Defines dhawu al-arham and presents intra-Hanafi dispute on ranking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the order of dhawu al-arham and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When no asabah or fixed-share heir exists, dhawu al-arham inherit, but their order is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This lesson examines the category of dhawu al-arham (maternal relatives) in Hanafi inheritance law. While they inherit in the absence of primary heirs, their order of precedence is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly distributing an estate when only distant relatives survive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of heirs
Understand who dhawu al-arham are and when they inherit (no asabah or ashab al-furud).
The Quranic basis is verse 33:6.
2
State the three positions
List Abu Hanifa's view (Zahir al-Riwayah: maternal grandfather first), his second narration (children of daughters first), and the Sahibayn's view (children of daughters first, then siblings' children, then maternal grandfather).
The difference is between considering ascendants vs. descendants as closer.
3
Analyze the proofs
Why does each side argue for their ranking? Abu Hanifa sees the maternal grandfather as an ascendant (usul); Sahibayn see children as direct descendants (far') and analogize to asabah rules.
4
Apply to a concrete case
Work through the example: deceased leaves maternal grandfather and daughter of brother from mother. Abu Hanifa gives all to grandfather; Sahibayn give all to brother's daughter.
This illustrates the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fuqaha (e.g., al-Quduri) adopt the view prioritizing children of daughters. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The second narration from Abu Hanifa also supports this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذوي الأرحام
وإذا لم تكن للميت عصبة، ولا ذو سهم : وَرِثه ذوو أرحامه عشرة : ولد البنت وولد الأخت، وبنت الأخ، وبنت العم، والخال والخالة، وأبو الأم، والعم من الأم، والعمة، وولد الأخ من الأم، ومن أدلى بهم.
والأصل في هذا أن ذوي الأرحام أولى بالميراث من بيت المال؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولُوا الْأَرْحَامِ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلَى بِبَعْضٍ فِي كِتَابِ اللَّهِ﴾ [الأحزاب/٦].
قوله: (وهم عشرة ولد البنت وولد الأخت، وبنتُ الأخ، وبنتُ العم، والخال والخاله، وأبو الأم، والعم من الأم، والعمة، وولد الأخ من الأم، ومن أدلى بهم).
ثم توريث ذوي الأرحام كتوريث العصبة، يرث الأقرب فالأقرب إلى الميت، إلا أن الكلام وقع في معرفة الأقرب وأولاهم : مَن كان من ولد الميت . ثم الجد أبو الأم. ثم ولد الأبوين، أو أحدهما، وهم بنات الإخوة، وولد الأخوات .
قال أبو حنيفة أقربهم إلى الميت الجد أبو الأم، ثم أولاد البنات، ثم أولاد الأخوات وبنات الإخوة، ثم العمات والخالات، ثم أولادهم، كذا ذكره في ظاهر الرواية.
وروي عنه: أن أقربهم: أولاد البنات، ثم الجد أبو الأم. وقالا : الأقرب: أولاد البنات، ثم أولاد الأخوات، وبنات الإخوة، ثم الجد أبو الأم، ثم العمات، والخالات، ثم أولادهن. كذا في «الخجندي». وفي القدوري: (وأولاهم : مَن كان من ولد الميت)؛ لأن ولد الميت أقرب إليه من غيره وإن سفل.
قوله : (ثم الجد أبو الأم، ثم ولد الأبوين أو أحدهما، وهم بنات الإخوة وأولاد الأخوات). يعني أنهم أولى من أولاد الجد، وهم العمات، ومَن شاكلهم من ذوي الرحم من أولاد الجد أبي الأم؛ لأن الإخوة أقرب إلى الميت من هؤلاء، فكذلك أولادهم أقرب إليه، كأولاد ابنه.
أما إذا ترك جده أبا أمه، وابنة أخيه لأمه: فالمال للجد أبي الأم عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : هو لابنة الأخ من الأم.
Chapter of Dhawu al-Arham. If the deceased has no asabah (agnatic heir) nor a fixed-share heir, his dhawu al-arham inherit, ten: child of a daughter, child of a sister, daughter of a brother, daughter of a paternal uncle, maternal uncle and maternal aunt, mother's father, paternal uncle from mother, paternal aunt, child of a brother from mother, and whoever is connected through them. The basis for this is that dhawu al-arham are more entitled to inheritance than the public treasury, due to His statement: 'And those of kinship are more entitled to one another in the Book of Allah' [al-Ahzab:6]. His statement: 'and they are ten...' Then the inheritance of dhawu al-arham is like the inheritance of asabah: the closest then the closest to the deceased inherits, except that discussion occurred about knowing who is closest. And the foremost among them is whoever is from the offspring of the deceased, then the maternal grandfather, then the children of both parents or one of them, who are daughters of brothers and children of sisters. Abu Hanifa said: The closest to the deceased is the maternal grandfather, then the children of daughters, then children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then paternal aunts and maternal aunts, then their children. Thus it is mentioned in the Zahir al-Riwayah. And it is narrated from him that the closest are children of daughters, then the maternal grandfather. And the two said: The closest are children of daughters, then children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then the maternal grandfather, then paternal aunts and maternal aunts, then their children. Thus in al-Khujandi. And in al-Quduri: 'And the foremost among them is whoever is from the offspring of the deceased,' because the offspring of the deceased are closer to him than others even if low in rank. His statement: 'then the maternal grandfather, then the children of both parents or one of them, who are daughters of brothers and children of sisters.' This means that they are more entitled than the children of the grandfather, who are paternal aunts and the like of dhawu al-arham from the children of the maternal grandfather, because siblings are closer to the deceased than these, so likewise their children are closer to him, like the children of his son. If the deceased leaves behind his maternal grandfather and the daughter of his brother from mother, then according to Abu Hanifa the wealth goes to the maternal grandfather. And the two said it goes to the mother's brother's daughter.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the order of precedence among dhawu al-arham (maternal relatives) in inheritance. The author begins by stating the general rule: when there is no asabah (agnatic heir) and no fixed-share heir (quranic heir), the dhawu al-arham inherit. This is based on the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Ahzab that gives precedence to kinship. The text lists ten categories of dhawu al-arham, including grandchildren through daughters, siblings' children, etc. Then it explains that inheritance among them follows closeness, but there is disagreement on how to determine closeness. The author presents three views: (1) According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, Abu Hanifa ranks the maternal grandfather first, then descendants of daughters, then descendants of siblings, then aunts/uncles. (2) Another narration from Abu Hanifa places children of daughters first. (3) The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rank children of daughters first, then children of siblings, then maternal grandfather, then aunts. The author then quotes al-Quduri that the foremost are those directly descending from the deceased (children of daughters). The commentary explains that siblings' children take precedence over aunts because siblings are closer than aunts to the deceased. A concrete case illustrates: when the deceased leaves a maternal grandfather and a daughter of a brother from mother, Abu Hanifa gives the entire inheritance to the maternal grandfather, while the two disciples give it to the brother's daughter. This difference stems from whether maternal grandfather is considered closer (Abu Hanifa) or the child of a sibling is considered closer (Sahibayn). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school varies, but later scholars tend to follow the view of the two disciples or the second narration from Abu Hanifa, as it aligns with al-Quduri's statement prioritizing direct descendants.

Key Terms
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): All blood relatives who are neither asabah (agnatic heirs) nor ashab al-furud (fixed-share heirs). They inherit in the absence of the formal heirs.
عصبة (asabah): Agnatic relatives who inherit the residue after fixed shares; typically male through males.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc. This is the primary transmitted position of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa (in the Zahir al-Riwayah), who is the closest among dhawu al-arham?
A) Children of daughters
B) Maternal grandfather
C) Children of sisters
D) Paternal aunts

Key Takeaway
The order of dhawu al-arham is disputed among Hanafi imams; the mufta bihi places children of daughters first, then siblings' children, then maternal grandfather, then aunts.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Priority of Dhawi al-Arham
Intra-Hanafi dispute on Dhawi al-Arham priority and distribution method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the priority order among Dhawi al-Arham and apply the distribution rules for children of daughters according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn prioritise children of daughters over the maternal grandfather; Abu Hanifa reverses the order — a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in faraid.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar outlines the inheritance order for Dhawi al-Arham (distant blood relatives). The matn gives a general order, but there is a well-known disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on who comes first: the maternal grandfather or the children of daughters. Beyond this, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad themselves differ on how to divide the estate among children of daughters — by counting individuals or by tracing the first generation. Understanding these disputes is essential for correct fatwa in cases where no closer heirs exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general order in the matn
The matn states: first those from the offspring of the deceased (children of daughters), then maternal grandfather, then children of brothers/sisters. This is the baseline.
2
Locate Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa places the maternal grandfather before children of daughters, with a variant narration putting children of daughters first. Note the contradiction in riwayah.
3
Locate the Sahibayn's position
They place children of daughters first, then children of sisters/daughters of brothers, then maternal grandfather. This is the mufta bihi.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the dispute to a case: deceased leaves a maternal grandfather and a daughter of a brother from the mother. Abu Hanifa gives to grandfather; Sahibayn give to brother's daughter. See the practical difference.
5
Examine the sub-dispute on distribution among children of daughters
Abu Yusuf counts the number of individuals (bodies) and divides per capita with male double female. Muhammad considers the first generation from the deceased: if that generation consists of individuals, same; if a single line, then inheritance passes as per the original share.
Work through the examples: daughter of a daughter and son of a daughter (same result); son of daughter of daughter and daughter of son of daughter (different result).

Source Text

باب ذوي الأرحام
وأولاهم : مَن كان من ولد الميت .
ثم الجد أبو الأم.
ثم ولد الأبوين، أو أحدهما، وهم بنات الإخوة، وولد الأخوات .
قال أبو حنيفة أقربهم إلى الميت الجد أبو الأم، ثم أولاد البنات، ثم أولاد الأخوات وبنات الإخوة، ثم العمات والخالات، ثم أولادهم، كذا ذكره في ظاهر الرواية.
وروي عنه: أن أقربهم: أولاد البنات، ثم الجد أبو الأم. وقالا : الأقرب: أولاد البنات، ثم أولاد الأخوات، وبنات الإخوة، ثم الجد أبو الأم، ثم العمات، والخالات، ثم أولادهن. كذا في «الخجندي». وفي القدوري»: (وأولاهم : مَن كان من ولد الميت)؛ لأن ولد الميت أقرب إليه من غيره وإن سفل.
قوله : (ثم الجد أبو الأم، ثم ولد الأبوين أو أحدهما، وهم بنات الإخوة وأولاد الأخوات.
يعني أنهم أولى من أولاد الجد، وهم العمات، ومَن شاكلهم من ذوي الرحم من أولاد الجد أبي الأم؛ لأن الإخوة أقرب إلى الميت من هؤلاء، فكذلك أولادهم أقرب إليه، كأولاد ابنه.
أما إذا ترك جده أبا أمه، وابنة أخيه لأمه: فالمال للجد أبي الأم عند أبي حنيفة.
وقالا : هو لابنة الأخ من الأم.
وكذلك روي عن أبي حنيفة في ابنة الأخت للأب والأم، أو للأب أن المال للجد أبي الأم ؛ لأن للجد أبي الأم ولاداً، فهو أولى مسائل : بنت بنت وابنُ بنتِ بنتِ : المال لبنتِ البنت؛ لأنها أقرب. - ابن بنت وبنت بنت أخرى، أو هما لبنت واحدة: فالمال بينهما، للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، كأنه ترك ابناً وبنتاً من صلبه. - قال الخُجَندي: الأصل في أولاد البنات عند أبي يوسف: أنه يُعتبر الأبدان، ويقسم بالأبدان : إن كانوا كلهم ذكوراً: فالمال بينهم بالسوية، وإن كانوا مختلطين: فالمال بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين.
ومحمد : يعتبر في أولاد البنات أول الخلاف، فإن كان أول الخلاف يقع بالأبدان : فإنه يكون بينهم، للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وإن كان الخلاف في الأصل: يعطي لهم ميراث الأصل.

وكذا عند محمد ؛ لأن أول الخلاف وقع بالأبدان.

Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. And the first of them: whoever is from the offspring of the deceased. Then the grandfather, the father of the mother. Then the children of both parents, or one of them, and they are the daughters of brothers and the children of sisters. Abu Hanifa said: The closest to the deceased is the grandfather, the father of the mother, then the children of daughters, then the children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then the paternal aunts and maternal aunts, then their children. Thus it is mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah. And it is narrated from him: that the closest are the children of daughters, then the grandfather, the father of the mother. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The closest are the children of daughters, then the children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then the grandfather, the father of the mother, then the paternal aunts and maternal aunts, then their children. Thus in al-Khujandi. And in al-Quduri: (And the first of them: whoever is from the offspring of the deceased); because the offspring of the deceased are closer to him than others even if lower in lineage. His statement: (Then the grandfather, the father of the mother, then the children of both parents or one of them, and they are the daughters of brothers and the children of sisters). Meaning they are more entitled than the children of the grandfather, and they are the paternal aunts and those similar from the Dhawi al-Arham of the children of the grandfather, the father of the mother; because brothers are closer to the deceased than these, so likewise their children are closer to him, like the children of his son. If he leaves his grandfather (father of his mother) and the daughter of his brother from the mother: the wealth belongs to the grandfather, father of the mother according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: it belongs to the daughter of the brother from the mother. And likewise it is narrated from Abu Hanifa concerning the daughter of the sister from the father and mother, or from the father, that the wealth belongs to the grandfather, father of the mother; because the grandfather, father of the mother, has children, so he is more entitled. Issues: Daughter of a daughter and son of a daughter of a daughter: the wealth belongs to the daughter of the daughter because she is closer. - Son of a daughter and another daughter of a daughter, or they are for one daughter: the wealth is between them, for the male the share of two females, as if he left a son and a daughter from his own loins. - Al-Khujandi said: The principle concerning the children of daughters according to Abu Yusuf is that the bodies are considered, and it is divided by bodies: if they are all males, the wealth is between them equally; if they are mixed, the wealth is between them, for the male the share of two females. And Muhammad considers in the children of daughters the first of the origin: if the first of the origin falls by bodies, then it is between them, for the male the share of two females; but if the origin is in the origin (i.e., the branch line), he gives them the inheritance of the origin. Explanation: If one leaves a daughter of a daughter and a son of a daughter, it is between them, for the male the share of two females. As for according to Abu Yusuf, it is not problematic because he considers the bodies, and one is male and the other female. Likewise according to Muhammad because the first of the origin falls by bodies. And if one leaves a son of a daughter of a daughter and a daughter of a son of a daughter, according to Abu Yusuf: the wealth...
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage discusses the order of inheritance among Dhawi al-Arham (distant blood relatives who are not ashab al-furud or asabah). The author first states the general order as per the matn of al-Quduri: the closest is whoever is from the offspring of the deceased (i.e., children of daughters), then the maternal grandfather (father of the mother), then children of both parents or one of them (i.e., daughters of brothers and children of sisters). He then explains the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Hanifa holds that the maternal grandfather is closer than the children of daughters, based on the principle that the maternal grandfather is connected to the deceased through his mother, and thus has a stronger link. However, there is also a narration from him that children of daughters take precedence. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that children of daughters are the closest, followed by children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then the maternal grandfather. This disagreement affects practical cases, such as when the deceased leaves a maternal grandfather and a daughter of a brother from the mother: Abu Hanifa gives the estate to the grandfather, while the Sahibayn give it to the brother's daughter. The passage then provides several examples illustrating the rules for children of daughters. According to Abu Yusuf, distribution among them is based on the number of individuals (bodies), with males receiving twice the share of females. According to Muhammad, one looks at the first generation from the deceased: if that generation consists of individuals (i.e., the immediate ancestors are individuals), it is divided per capita; if the first generation is a line (e.g., a single child), then the inheritance passes according to the original share.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arhaam): Relatives who are neither Quranic share holders (ashab al-furud) nor residuary successors (asabah); they inherit only if the others are absent.
الجد أبو الأم (al-jadd abu al-umm): The maternal grandfather, i.e., the father of the mother of the deceased.
أولاد البنات (awlad al-banaat): Children of the deceased's daughters, i.e., grandchildren through the daughter line.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), who is the closest among the following Dhawi al-Arham?
A) The maternal grandfather (father of the mother)
B) Children of daughters (grandchildren)
C) Daughters of brothers
D) The father's aunts

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi faraid, the relied-upon order of Dhawi al-Arham places children of daughters first, then children of sisters/daughters of brothers, then maternal grandfather, then aunts and their children.

Inheritance of Daughters of Sisters and Aunts/Uncles (Dhawi al-Arham) – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance of daughters of sisters and rule for aunts/uncles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on inheritance of children of sisters and distinguish between the per stirpes method of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and the proximity method of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
For Dhawi al-Arham, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad give per stirpes shares; Abu Yusuf gives all to the nearest relative.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah deals with the inheritance of Dhawi al-Arham, specifically children of sisters, aunts, and uncles. There is a known intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani distribute based on the shares of the connecting maternal line (per stirpes), while Abu Yusuf applies the principle of proximity (aqrab fa aqrab). Understanding this difference is key to applying Hanafi inheritance rules for distant relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs
The case involves three daughters of sisters: one from a full sister, one from a consanguine sister, and one from a uterine sister. These are Dhawi al-Arham.
2
Apply the Abu Hanifa/Muhammad method
Give each child the share that their mother would have received if alive: full sister's child gets half, consanguine sister's child gets one-sixth (to complete two-thirds with the full sister's child), uterine sister's child gets one-sixth.
These are the fixed shares of the sisters themselves.
3
Calculate distribution with radd
After assigning fixed shares (½ + ⅙ + ⅙ = 5/6), the remaining 1/6 is returned proportionally, making the total denominator 5. Under radd, the full sister's child gets 3/5, consanguine sister's child gets 1/5, uterine sister's child gets 1/5.
The calculation: ½ = 2.5/5, ⅙ = 0.833.../5, etc., but the text simplifies to 5 shares overall.
4
Compare with Abu Yusuf's method
Abu Yusuf gives the entire estate to the child of the full sister because she is the nearest relative, excluding the others entirely.
Abu Yusuf uses 'aqrab fa aqrab' (nearest then nearer).
5
Understand rules for aunts/uncles and their children
For aunts/uncles themselves, there is consensus to prefer the nearest. For their children, Abu Yusuf divides per capita (by heads), while Muhammad (and Abu Hanifa) apply the same per stirpes method as for children of sisters.
Example: among three maternal aunts (full, consanguine, uterine), the full aunt takes all by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذوي الأرحام
وإن ترك ثلاث بنات أخوات متفرقات، أو ثلاثة بني أخوات متفرقات: فالأصل عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد: أنهم يُعطون ميراث أصلهم: لأولاد الأخوات من الأب والأم النصف، ولأولاد الأخوات من الأب: السدس تكملة الثلثين، ولأولاد الأخوات من الأم: السدس ميراث أصلهم، والباقي رَدُّ عليهم على قدر أنصبائهم، فيكون بينهم على خمسة. وقال أبو يوسف: الميراثُ لولد الأخت للأب والأم؛ لأنه يعتبر الأقرب فالأقرب، وهما يعتبران بمَن تُدلي به كل واحدة منهن، فجعل لكل واحدة ما كان لأمها.
وأما العمات والخالات : فإنه يُعتبر فيهن الأقرب فالأقرب بالإجماع. وأما أولادهن : فعلى قول أبي يوسف: يُقسم بالأبدان، وعند محمد: كما ذكرنا في أولاد البنات، وأولاد الأخوات.
بيانه: ثلاث خالات متفرقات : المال للخالة من قبل الأب والأم، إجماعاً؛ لأنها أقرب.
والأم.

ولو ترك خالاً وخالةً كلاهما في درجة واحدة : فالمالُ بينهما، للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين.
Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. And if he leaves three daughters of sisters who are different types (full, consanguine, uterine), or three sons of sisters who are different types: the principle according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that they are given the inheritance of their origin (mother): for children of the full sister half, for children of the consanguine sister one-sixth as completion of two-thirds, for children of the uterine sister one-sixth as the inheritance of their origin, and the remainder is returned to them proportionally to their shares, so it becomes among them on five shares. And Abu Yusuf said: the inheritance is for the child of the full sister; because he considers the nearest and nearer, while they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) consider the one through whom each one traces relationship, so he made for each one what was for her mother. As for paternal aunts and maternal aunts: the nearest and nearer is considered among them by consensus. And as for their children: according to Abu Yusuf, it is divided by heads, and according to Muhammad, as we mentioned regarding children of daughters and children of sisters. Explanation: three maternal aunts of different types: the entire wealth is for the maternal aunt from father and mother (full), by consensus, because she is the nearest. And if he leaves three maternal uncles of different types: the entire wealth is for the maternal uncle from father (full). And if he leaves a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt both at the same degree: the wealth is between them, for the male the like of the share of two females.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah discusses inheritance rules for Dhawi al-Arham (distant relatives who are not Quranic heirs or residuaries), specifically children of sisters, aunts, and uncles. The text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that each child of a sister inherits the same share as their mother would have if she were alive—full sister's child gets half, consanguine sister's child gets one-sixth (to complete two-thirds together with the full sister's child), uterine sister's child gets one-sixth. After assigning these fixed shares, any surplus is returned (radd) proportionally, resulting in a denominator of five. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, applies the principle of 'aqrab fa aqrab' (nearest then nearer), giving all inheritance to the child of the full sister, considering her to be the closest relative. For aunts and uncles, the text states that there is consensus (ijma) on preferring the nearest. For their children, Abu Yusuf divides per capita (by heads), while Muhammad (implied with Abu Hanifa) applies the same method as for children of sisters, i.e., per stirpes. Specific examples show that among three maternal aunts, the full aunt takes all; among three maternal uncles, the full maternal uncle takes all; and if a maternal uncle and maternal aunt are at the same degree, the estate is divided with the male getting twice the share of the female.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (Dhawi al-Arham): Distant relatives who are neither Quranic heirs (ashab al-fara'id) nor residuaries (asabat), such as children of sisters, aunts, uncles, etc.
أقرب فالأقرب (Aqrab fa aqrab): The principle of preferring the nearest relative in degree over more distant ones; used by Abu Yusuf in Dhawi al-Arham inheritance.
رَدّ (Radd): Return of the surplus of an estate to the Quranic heirs proportionally, after their fixed shares have been given, when there is no residuary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man dies leaving three daughters of different types of sisters (full sister's daughter, consanguine sister's daughter, uterine sister's daughter), according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the share of the full sister's daughter before radd?
A) All the inheritance
B) Half
C) One-sixth
D) Two-thirds

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance for children of sisters, the mufta bihi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) distributes per stirpes according to the mother's share with proportional radd, while Abu Yusuf's proximity principle gives all to the nearest child.

Inheritance of Aunts, Uncles, and Their Children: Per Stirpes vs Per Capita in Dhawi al-Arham
Specific inheritance rules for aunts/uncles and children, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for inheritance among aunts, uncles, and their children, and distinguish between Abu Yusuf's per capita method and Muhammad's per stirpes method in the case of children of maternal uncle and maternal aunt.

Core Idea
Among Dhawi al-Arham, inheritance passes by representation (per stirpes) according to Imam Muhammad, not per capita.

This lesson continues our study of Dhawi al-Arham (uterine relatives) inheritance, focusing on the shares of paternal and maternal aunts, uncles, and their children. The text presents a series of specific cases, including a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on how the children of a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt should inherit. Understanding this dispute reveals the school's underlying principle: inheritance by representation (per stirpes) for Dhawi al-Arham, where each child steps into the share of their parent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Note the rule: when two persons are equal in degree (distance from the deceased), the one who traces through an actual heir (adla bi-warith) is preferred. This is illustrated by the daughter of a paternal uncle (who is a residuary) versus the son of a paternal aunt (a Dhawi al-Arham).
This rule helps resolve conflicts among distant relatives.
2
Apply the Rules for Aunts and Uncles
Work through the examples: three paternal aunts (full, consanguine, uterine) – the full aunt takes all. Between a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt, the paternal aunt gets 2/3, maternal aunt 1/3. Similarly, between a paternal aunt and a maternal uncle, the aunt gets 2/3 and the uncle 1/3. Notice that the paternal side is favored over the maternal side with a 2:1 ratio.
The ratio appears to be derived from the general principle that males inherit twice females, but here the aunt is female yet gets double the maternal uncle? Actually, it's because the paternal aunt is closer in degree and also from a stronger side.
3
Analyze the Dispute: Check the Children Case
Focus on the case: a man dies leaving a daughter of a maternal uncle and a son of a maternal aunt. Abu Yusuf says divide per capita with the male getting twice the female (son gets 2/3, daughter gets 1/3). Muhammad says each child inherits the share of their parent: the daughter takes the maternal uncle's share (which would be 2/3 if he were alive) and the son takes the maternal aunt's share (1/3). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
This dispute hinges on whether children represent their parents or inherit as independent individuals.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
According to the Hanafi school, Imam Muhammad's view (per stirpes) is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, as it is the Zahir al-Riwayah. This is because the principle of representation is consistently applied for Dhawi al-Arham.
Always check the hierarchy: Zahir al-Riwayah texts (Muhammad) take precedence over Nawadir (Abu Yusuf's view).
5
Practice with the Given Examples
Go through each scenario in the passage and calculate the shares under the mufta bihi position. For instance, for a paternal aunt and a maternal uncle, the aunt gets 2/3 and the uncle 1/3. Understand why the maternal uncle gets only 1/3 despite being male: because he is from the weaker maternal side.
These examples are testbeds for mastering the rules.

Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. And if two children of a father are equal in degree, then the most deserving of them is the one who traces relationship through an heir. And if he leaves three paternal aunts of different types: all the wealth is for the full paternal aunt (from father and mother), because she is the nearest. And if he leaves a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt: for the paternal aunt two-thirds, and for the maternal aunt one-third. For example, a man dies leaving a daughter of a paternal uncle and a son of a paternal aunt: all the wealth is for the daughter of the paternal uncle. Likewise, if he leaves a daughter of a daughter's daughter and a daughter of a son's daughter: the wealth is for the daughter of the son's daughter.

Commentary
This passage continues the rules of Dhawi al-Arham, now focusing on aunts (paternal and maternal), uncles, and their children. It begins with a general rule: if two persons are of the same degree (distance from the deceased), the one who traces relationship through an actual heir (warith) is given preference. For example, a daughter of a paternal uncle is from the residuary class (asaba), whereas a son of a paternal aunt is from Dhawi al-Arham, so the former takes all. Then specific cases: among three paternal aunts (full, consanguine, uterine), the full paternal aunt gets all by consensus. Between a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt, the paternal aunt inherits two-thirds (since she is from the paternal side, which is stronger) and the maternal aunt gets one-third. Between a paternal aunt and a maternal uncle, the paternal aunt gets two-thirds and the maternal uncle one-third. If a maternal aunt and the son of a paternal aunt are present, the maternal aunt inherits because she is closer in degree (the son is one step further). A key dispute appears between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning the children of maternal uncle and maternal aunt: Abu Yusuf divides the estate per capita (2:1 male:female ratio), whereas Muhammad gives each the share of their parent (daughter of maternal uncle takes the maternal uncle's share, i.e., one-third? Actually maternal uncle gets one-third? Wait: in earlier cases, maternal uncle gets one-third when with paternal aunt. Here, daughter of maternal uncle and son of maternal aunt: parent of daughter is maternal uncle, parent of son is maternal aunt. Since maternal uncle and maternal aunt are both Dhawi al-Arham, but their shares differ? The text says: according to Muhammad, the daughter inherits two-thirds (the share of the maternal uncle as a male? Or because the maternal uncle's share is two-thirds? Actually earlier, maternal uncle with paternal aunt gets one-third, but maternal aunt with paternal aunt gets one-third? Let's check: Earlier: paternal aunt + maternal aunt: paternal aunt 2/3, maternal aunt 1/3. Paternal aunt + maternal uncle: paternal aunt 2/3, maternal uncle 1/3. So maternal uncle and maternal aunt each get 1/3 when with a paternal aunt. But in this case, there is no paternal aunt, only the children. So the parents' shares would be: maternal uncle would have gotten 1/3? But that seems arbitrary. Actually, the method of Muhammad: each child inherits the share of their parent in the hypothetical distribution of the estate among the parents. So we need to consider the scenario: if the parents (maternal uncle and maternal aunt) were alive, how would the estate be divided? According to the earlier rules, if there are only a maternal uncle and a maternal aunt (no other heirs), the male gets twice the female? The text earlier said: if a maternal uncle and maternal aunt are at the same degree, the estate is divided 2:1. So the maternal uncle would get 2/3 and the maternal aunt 1/3. Then the daughter of maternal uncle inherits her father's share (2/3) and the son of maternal aunt inherits his mother's share (1/3). That matches Muhammad's ruling: two-thirds for the daughter, one-third for the son. Abu Yusuf, however, simply divides per capita with the 2:1 ratio, so the son being male gets twice the daughter? Actually, per capita: total 3 shares, male gets 2, female gets 1, so son gets 2/3, daughter gets 1/3. That is the opposite. So the dispute is significant. The passage then gives a final example: daughter of paternal uncle vs son of paternal aunt: the daughter takes all because she is from the residuary line. The commentary (sharh) wraps up with a quote from the matn regarding preferring the one who traces relationship through an heir.

Key Terms
عمة (Ammah): Paternal aunt (father's sister). In inheritance, classified as Dhawi al-Arham.
خالة (Khalah): Maternal aunt (mother's sister). Classified as Dhawi al-Arham.
أدلى بوارث (Adla bi-warith): Traces relationship through an heir (i.e., a person who is a Quranic heir or residuary). This gives preference among those equal in degree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man dies leaving a daughter of his maternal uncle and a son of his maternal aunt. According to the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school, what are their shares?
A) Daughter gets 1/3, son gets 2/3
B) Daughter gets 2/3, son gets 1/3
C) Daughter gets 1/2, son gets 1/2
D) Daughter gets 1/4, son gets 3/4

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance for Dhawi al-Arham, children inherit the share of their parent (per stirpes), not per capita, and the paternal side is preferred over the maternal side with a 2:1 ratio when degrees are equal.

Preference Among Dhawu al-Arham Heirs Equal in Degree
States the rule and provides multiple worked examples with internal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of preference among dhawu al-arham heirs of the same degree by identifying the connecting relative's status as an heir, and resolve the internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad for specific cases.

Core Idea
Among dhawu al-arham of equal degree, the heir linked through a recognized inheritor takes priority.

Inheritance of dhawu al-arham (maternal relatives and non-agnatic connections) follows a specific hierarchy in the Hanafi school. When two such relatives are equally distant from the deceased (same degree), the one whose connecting relative (the person through whom they claim) is a legal heir (e.g., father, son) is preferred over one whose connecting relative is not an heir. This rule ensures that inheritance flows through recognized channels. The passage from Mukhtasar al-Quduri, with commentary, illustrates this with several examples and records an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and their degrees
List all dhawu al-arham heirs and count the steps between each heir and the deceased through the connecting relative. Two heirs are equal in degree if they are the same number of steps away.
Degree = number of generations between the heir and the deceased, counting through the connecting relative.
2
Determine the connecting relative's status
For each heir, identify the connecting relative (the person through whom the heir claims). Check whether that connecting relative is a recognized heir (asabah or fixed-share heir) in the presence of the current deceased.
A connecting relative who is an asabah or a Quranic sharer makes the dhawu al-arham heir 'adla bi-warith' (linked through an heir).
3
Apply the preference rule
If both heirs are equal in degree, the one whose connecting relative is an heir (adla bi-warith) takes precedence. If only one is linked through an heir, that heir receives the entire estate. If both are linked through an heir, compare their degrees; if still equal, the general inheritance rule of male double female applies (or the specific method of Muhammad).
The text gives examples: paternal aunt (linked through father, an heir) versus maternal aunt (linked through mother, not an heir): paternal aunt gets 2/3, maternal aunt 1/3.
4
Resolve the internal Hanafi dispute
For the case of a daughter of a maternal uncle and a son of a maternal aunt, the text records two views: Abu Yusuf applies the male:female 2:1 ratio (general inheritance principle), while Muhammad assigns shares based on the connecting relative's hypothetical inheritance (daughter of maternal uncle takes 2/3 as if her mother inherited, son of maternal aunt takes 1/3 as if his mother inherited). The mufta bihi (relied-upon view) is that of Abu Yusuf, as it aligns with qiyas.
This dispute does not affect the outcome in this specific case (both give son 1/3), but may differ in other scenarios.

وإن ترك ثلاث عمات متفرقات : فالمالُ كله للعمة من قبل الأب والأم؛ لأنها أقرب.
ولو ترك عمة وخالةً للعمة الثلثان، وللخالة الثلث.

Section on Dhawu al-Arham. And if two children of a father are equal in degree, then the most entitled of them is the one who is linked through an heir. And if he leaves three maternal aunts who are separate: then all the wealth is for the paternal aunt from the father and mother; because she is closer. And if he leaves a paternal aunt and a maternal aunt: for the paternal aunt two-thirds, and for the maternal aunt one-third. And if he leaves a paternal aunt and a maternal uncle: one-third for the maternal uncle, and two-thirds for the paternal aunt. And if he leaves a maternal aunt and a son of a paternal aunt: the wealth is for the maternal aunt; because the son of the paternal aunt is farther in degree. And if he leaves a daughter of a maternal uncle and a son of a maternal aunt: according to Abu Yusuf: the wealth is between them, for the male the share of two females. And according to Muhammad: two-thirds for the daughter of the maternal uncle, and one-third for the son of the maternal aunt, each inherits the inheritance of his origin. And if he leaves a daughter of a paternal uncle and a son of a paternal aunt: all the wealth is for the daughter of the paternal uncle; because she is from the children of the asabah, and the other is from the children of dhawu al-arham. * His saying: (And if two heirs are equal in one degree): then the most entitled of them is the one linked through an heir, like a man who dies leaving a daughter of a paternal uncle and a son of a paternal aunt: all the wealth is for the daughter of the paternal uncle. And similarly if he leaves a daughter of a daughter's daughter and a daughter of a son's daughter: the wealth is for the daughter of the son's daughter. (1) And in some copies of al-Quduri: 'And if two children of a father are equal in degree.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the matn (Mukhtasar al-Quduri) on the inheritance of dhawu al-arham (maternal relatives and non-agnatic connections). The key principle is stated first: when two dhawu al-arham are equal in degree (i.e., same number of steps from the deceased), the one whose connecting relative is a recognized heir (e.g., a paternal uncle who is an asabah) takes priority over one whose connecting relative is not an heir. For example, a paternal aunt (father's sister) is preferred over a maternal aunt (mother's sister) because the paternal aunt is linked through the father, who is an heir, whereas the maternal aunt is linked through the mother, who is not an heir in the presence of a father. The commentary clarifies this with examples, including a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the case of a daughter of a maternal uncle and a son of a maternal aunt: Abu Yusuf applies the principle of inheritance for dhawu al-arham (treating them as heirs for their connecting relative), giving the male double the female, while Muhammad assigns the daughter of the maternal uncle two-thirds and the son of the maternal aunt one-third, based on each tracing back to their root (the maternal uncle and maternal aunt, respectively). The relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf. The footnote notes a variant reading in some copies of al-Quduri.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arḥām): Relatives who are neither asabah (agnatic) nor owners of fixed shares; they inherit only when no asabah or fixed-share heir exists. Includes maternal relatives, daughters' children, etc.
أَدْلى بوارث (adlā bi-wārith): Linked through an heir: a dhawu al-arham whose connecting relative (the person between them and the deceased) is a recognized heir (asabah or fixed-share heir).
ولد أب (walad ab): Literally 'children of a father'; in this context, it refers to descendants of a common paternal ancestor, meaning paternal relatives. However, the commentary interprets it as 'two heirs' (وارثان).
عمة (ʿamma): Paternal aunt (father's sister). In dhawu al-arham, she is favored over maternal relatives because her connecting relative (father) is an asabah.
خالة (khāla): Maternal aunt (mother's sister).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The maternal aunt gets all because she is closer in degree.
D) The paternal aunt gets all because she is linked through an heir.

Key Takeaway
When two dhawu al-arham are equal in degree, the one whose connecting relative is a recognized heir takes precedence, and the mufta bihi for distribution follows the general inheritance ratio (male:female 2:1).

Inheritance of Abu al-Umm, Mu'tiq, and Mawla al-Muwalat
States rulings on dhawu al-arham, mu'tiq, and mawla al-muwalat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for dhawu al-arham priority, the mu'tiq's right to surplus, and the inheritance of mawla al-muwalat, and apply them to simple cases.

Core Idea
Among dhawu al-arham, Abu al-Umm is the closest; mu'tiq takes residue; mawla al-muwalat inherits by contract.

This lesson covers three distinct inheritance categories in Hanafi faraid. First, the ordering of dhawu al-arham (distant relatives) where closeness determines priority. Second, the right of the manumitter (mu'tiq) to any residue after fixed shares. Third, the unique institution of mawla al-muwalat—inheritance by contract—and the Hanafi-Maliki disagreement. A specific example illustrates how closeness among mawla relatives determines the share.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three inheritance categories
The passage discusses three distinct groups: dhawu al-arham (distant blood relatives), the mu'tiq (manumitter), and mawla al-muwalat (client by contract). Note each one's basis: blood, slavery/wala', and contract respectively.
2
Order the dhawu al-arham sequence
According to Abu Hanifa, the closest dhawu al-arham is the maternal grandfather (Abu al-Umm), then children of daughters, then children of sisters and daughters of brothers, then paternal and maternal aunts, then their children. Memorize this hierarchy.
3
Understand mu'tiq's entitlement
The mu'tiq is entitled to the surplus (fadil) after fixed-share holders (dhawu al-furud) have taken their shares, but only if no other asabah (agnatic heir) exists. This is based on the wala' of emancipation.
4
Analyze mawla al-muwalat inheritance
Mawla al-muwalat is a contractual relationship where a convert enters an inheritance contract with a Muslim. According to Hanafi (by istihsan), he inherits if the deceased has no other heir. Imam Malik rejects this, sending the wealth to the treasury.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Apply the intra-Hanafi example
The text gives an example: if the mu'tiq (deceased) leaves the father of his mawla and the son of his mawla, the wealth goes to the son. The phrase 'عندهما' indicates this ruling is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, not necessarily Abu Hanifa.

And the closest of them is more entitled than the farther one, and the grandfather of the mother (Abu al-Umm) is more entitled than the sibling's child and the sister's child. And the manumitter (mu'tiq) is most entitled to the surplus of the share of the fixed-share holders if there is no asabah other than him. And the mawla al-muwalat inherits. And if the manumitter leaves a father of his mawla and a son of his mawla: his wealth is for the son. * His saying: (And the closest of them is more entitled than the farther one). And the mawla al-muwalat inherits), and he is a man who embraces Islam at the hand of a man, and becomes his mawla (client), and contracts with him, then dies and has no heir other than him: his inheritance is for him according to us. And Malik said (1): his inheritance is for the Muslims. 'Indahuma (according to both of them). His saying: (And if the manumitter leaves a father of his mawla and a son of his mawla: his wealth is for the son).
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of dhawu al-arham inheritance. It begins by stating the general rule: the closer dhawu al-arham excludes the farther. Then it specifies that Abu al-Umm (maternal grandfather) takes priority over children of siblings (e.g., nephew from brother/sister). The commentary lists the order of precedence according to Abu Hanifa: (1) Abu al-Umm, (2) children of daughters, (3) children of sisters and daughters of brothers, (4) paternal aunts and maternal aunts, (5) their children. Next, the text discusses the mu'tiq (the manumitter of a slave): if the freed slave dies without asabah or fixed-share heirs, the mu'tiq takes the surplus after fixed shares. If no such surplus, the mu'tiq gets nothing? Actually, 'المعتق أحق بالفاضل عن سهم ذوي السهام' means he is entitled to what remains after fixed shares. Then, mawla al-muwalat is a person who becomes a client through a contract of mutual inheritance (not through slavery). According to the Hanafis, such a mawla inherits from the person he contracted with if the latter has no other heir. The commentary explains the contract: a man embraces Islam at the hands of another, makes a contract of mutual inheritance, and then dies without any heir; his inheritance goes to the mawla al-muwalat. Imam Malik disagreed, saying it goes to the Muslim treasury. The text ends with an example: if the mu'tiq dies leaving the father of his mawla and the son of his mawla, the son inherits (as the closer relative). The phrase 'عندهما' may indicate that this view is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (as opposed to Abu Hanifa).

Key Terms
أبو الأم (abū al-umm): Maternal grandfather; the father of the mother. In dhawu al-arham, he is considered the closest relative and inherits first among them according to Abu Hanifa.
المعتق (al-muʿtiq): The manumitter of a slave; the person who freed a slave. He inherits from the freed slave as a kind of patron (mawla al-'itaq).
مولى الموالاة (mawlā al-muwālāt): A person who enters into a contract of mutual inheritance with another, typically when a non-Muslim embraces Islam at the hands of a Muslim and they agree to inherit from each other. Not based on slavery.
عندهما (ʿindahumā): According to them two (usually Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Indicates that the preceding ruling is their view, not necessarily Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Muslim treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
B) The mawla al-muwalat.
C) The closest dhawu al-arham.
D) The mu'tiq if he is also a freed slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes three unique inheritance causes: dhawu al-arham with a specific order, mu'tiq taking residue, and mawla al-muwalat by contract, with the latter disputed by Maliki.

The Order of Dhawi al-Arham in Hanafi Inheritance
Commentary on order of Dhawi al-Arham and preference of maternal grandfather

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of Dhawi al-Arham and identify who inherits first in a given set of distant relatives.

Core Idea
Among Dhawi al-Arham, proximity determines inheritance order: maternal grandfather is closest, then grandchildren through daughters, etc.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the ranking of relatives who are neither Quranic sharers nor agnate residuaries (Dhawi al-Arham). Understanding this order is crucial for calculating inheritance when no primary heirs exist, as per Abu Hanifa's school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of Dhawi al-Arham
Recognise that Dhawi al-Arham are relatives who are not Quranic sharers (e.g., spouse, parents, children) nor male agnate residuaries (e.g., paternal uncles). They inherit only when no residuaries exist.
This category is specific to Abu Hanifa's view; other schools may classify some of these as residuaries.
2
Memorise the fixed order from closest to most distant
The text gives the order: 1) Maternal grandfather (abu al-umm), 2) Children of daughters, 3) Children of sisters and daughters of brothers, 4) Paternal aunts and maternal aunts, 5) Their children.
This sequence is based on strength of lineage connection.
3
Apply the principle of proximity
Given a set of Dhawi al-Arham relatives, determine the closest one according to this order. That relative excludes all more distant ones (hajb).
For example, a maternal grandfather excludes all other Dhawi al-Arham, including a nephew (child of a brother/sister).
4
Note the specific rule about maternal grandfather
The text singles out that the maternal grandfather is preferred over the child of a brother or sister, confirming his top position.
This clarifies that even a close distant relative like a nephew does not outrank the maternal grandfather.

The Inheritance of the Manumitter and the Client of Muwalat
Two inheritance rulings on wala' with a madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the inheritance rights of the manumitter (mu'tiq) and the client of muwalat (mawla al-muwalat), and explain the difference with the Maliki school on muwalat.

Core Idea
The manumitter takes the residue; the client of muwalat inherits by contract, but Malik disagrees.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains two inheritance scenarios related to wala' (clientage). First, the manumitter (mu'tiq) is entitled to the residual estate after the Quranic sharers, if no other residuary exists. Second, if a person embraces Islam and enters a contract of muwalat with a Muslim, that Muslim inherits from him if he dies without any other heir. The Hanafi school (عندنا) holds this view, while Imam Malik holds that such inheritance goes to the Muslim public treasury. Let us examine the legal reasoning and practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, state the ruling for the mu'tiq: he is more entitled to the surplus (fadil) after the Quranic sharers, if no other residuary exists. Second, the ruling for mawla al-muwalat: the patron inherits from the client by virtue of their contract.
The mu'tiq's right is based on wala' al-itq (clientage of emancipation); mawla al-muwalat's right is based on a contractual agreement.
2
Understand the Hanafi basis
The Hanafi school considers the contract of muwalat as a valid cause (sabab) for inheritance. This is derived from the practice of the Companions and the principle that a Muslim can choose to create inheritance ties through contract.
The text uses 'عندنا' indicating the established view of the Hanafi imams.
3
Compare with the Maliki position
Imam Malik held that without blood or marriage, the estate goes to the Muslim public treasury (bayt al-mal). His evidence is that inheritance is restricted to relationships specified in the Quran and Sunnah, and muwalat is not among them.
The Maliki view is based on a strict interpretation of the sources.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, a specific individual (the patron) inherits. In the Maliki school, the public treasury receives the estate, which benefits the community at large.
This affects estate planning and the rights of converts.
5
Note intra-Hanafi consensus
The phrase 'عندهما' at the end of the passage refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, indicating they agreed with Abu Hanifa on this ruling. Thus there is no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this issue.
This strengthens the authority of the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمعتق أحقُّ بالفاضل عن سهم ذوي السهام، إذا لم تكن عصبة سواه. ومولى الموالاة يَرِثُ)، وهو الرجل يُسلم على يد رجل، ويواليه، ويعاقده، ثم يموت ولا وارث له غيره : فميراثه له عندنا. وقال مالك (۱) : ميراثه للمسلمين. عندهما. (۱) الإشراف للقاضي عبد الوهاب ٩٩٤/٢.

He is a man who embraces Islam at the hands of a man, and enters into muwalat with him, and contracts with him. Then he dies and has no heir other than him: so his inheritance is for him according to us. And Malik said: his inheritance is for the Muslims. According to both (i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). (1) Al-Ishraf by al-Qadi 'Abd al-Wahhab 2/994.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two matn rulings. First: the manumitter (mu'tiq) is entitled to the residue (fadil) of the estate after the Quranic sharers have taken their shares, provided there is no male agnate residuary (asabah) other than him. This is because the wala' (clientage) of emancipation creates a relationship of inheritance. Second: the client of muwalat (mawla al-muwalat) inherits from his patron. Muwalat is a contract where a non-Muslim embraces Islam at the hands of a Muslim, enters into a pact of mutual inheritance, and then dies without any other heir. According to the Hanafi school, the Muslim patron inherits from him. Imam Malik disagreed, holding that the inheritance goes to the Muslim public treasury (bayt al-mal). The phrase 'عندهما' likely refers to the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, indicating that they agreed with Abu Hanifa on this ruling.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The manumitter; the person who frees a slave.
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalat): A person who embraces Islam at the hands of a Muslim and contracts mutual inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person embraces Islam at someone's hand and enters muwalat, then dies without any heir, who inherits?
A) The Muslim public treasury
B) The person who accepted his Islam
C) His relatives
D) No one

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes inheritance through muwalat, a contract of mutual inheritance, while the Maliki school does not.

Inheritance of Client's Father and Son: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on client inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the inheritance of a client's father and son, explain Abu Yusuf's objection, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between the two views.

Core Idea
In wala' inheritance, the son of the client prevails over the father, unlike blood inheritance.

This passage presents a concise intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of the estate of a deceased client (mawla) when he leaves his father and his son. The matn states the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa, while Imam Abu Yusuf offers a partial amendment. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to trace how usul principles—particularly the rules of wala' versus qarabah (blood kinship)—yield different fiqh outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling given in the matn: 'the wealth goes to the son'. This is the position of Abu Hanifa.
The matn often represents the relied-upon view.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Abu Yusuf disagrees: he gives the father one-sixth and the remainder to the son.
Abu Yusuf is among the Sahibayn, so this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Trace the underlying principle
Reason: Abu Hanifa treats wala' as a relationship that passes exclusively to the son, not shared with the father. Abu Yusuf analogizes to the inheritance of relatives (where the father inherits a fixed share).
The exact dalil is not quoted, but we infer the usul reasoning.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the son gets the whole estate; under Abu Yusuf, the father gets 1/6.
This affects real inheritance distributions.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The matn gives Abu Hanifa's view, so it is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position. Later works confirm this as the stronger opinion.
Always check later fatwa sources for contemporary practice.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Client's Grandfather and Brother, and Prohibition on Selling Wala'
Dispute over inheritance between grandfather and brother, and rule that wala' is inalienable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on inheritance between a client's grandfather and brother, and explain why wala' cannot be sold or gifted.

Core Idea
Wala' is like lineage—inalienable; who inherits it between grandfather and brother splits the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the inheritance of a client's grandfather and brother. It also reiterates a foundational rule: wala' (clientage) is a bond akin to lineage, making it unsellable and non-transferable. Understanding these positions clarifies how Hanafi jurists extend their usul to faraid cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and the dispute
The client dies leaving a grandfather (his master's father) and a brother (his master's brother). The dispute is over who inherits the wala' rights.
Wala' follows inheritance rules but is not inherited itself.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its usul basis
Abu Hanifa holds that all goes to the grandfather, because his general principle is that brothers do not inherit anything when there is a grandfather.
This is based on his usul in faraid: the grandfather blocks brothers completely.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its usul basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the inheritance is divided equally between grandfather and brother, because their principle is that brothers share with the grandfather.
They consider brothers as heirs alongside the grandfather.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the brother gets nothing; under Sahibayn, the brother gets half.
The text does not specify a preferred view.
5
State the rule on wala' and its rationale
Wala' cannot be sold or gifted because it is a bond like lineage (nasab), which is also inalienable.
This rule is agreed upon in the Hanafi school.

Commentary
This passage covers two intra-Hanafi disputes and a final rule. First, regarding the inheritance of a client's grandfather and brother: Abu Hanifa gives all to the grandfather, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) say it is divided equally between them. The reasoning is given: Abu Hanifa's general principle is that brothers do not inherit anything when there is a grandfather, so he applies the same rule to wala'. The Sahibayn, on the other hand, hold that brothers share with the grandfather in inheritance, so they apply that principle to wala'. Second, the text reiterates the prohibition on selling or gifting wala', explaining that wala' is a bond like that of lineage (nasab), which cannot be sold, gifted, or inherited. This is a foundational rule of Hanafi fiqh.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Inheritance of Wala': Grandfather vs Brother
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on inheritance of wala' when a freed slave leaves a grandfather and a brother, and state the Hanafi rule that wala' is non-transferable.

Core Idea
Wala' is like lineage: it cannot be sold, gifted, or inherited, but its inheritance distribution follows the same intra-Hanafi dispute as lineage inheritance between grandfather and brother.

This passage from the chapter on Dhawil Arham presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, regarding who inherits the wala' of a freed slave. The disagreement arises from their differing positions on whether a grandfather excludes a brother in lineage inheritance. Understanding this dispute reveals how analogy (qiyas) operates in Hanafi faraid, and how wala' is treated as a bond akin to nasab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
The scenario: a freed slave dies leaving a grandfather (father's father) and a brother (both freedmen in the same wala' chain). Abu Hanifa holds that the grandfather takes all wala' inheritance; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it is divided between them.
The text also includes a separate ruling from Abu Yusuf about father and son, but the main dispute is about grandfather and brother.
2
Trace the basis of Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa analogizes to his principle in lineage inheritance: the grandfather excludes brothers entirely (hajb hirman). Since wala' is like lineage, the same rule applies.
This is an application of qiyas: the 'illa is the bond (luhmah) shared with lineage.
3
Trace the basis of the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad analogize to their own principle in lineage inheritance: brothers share with the grandfather. Therefore, in wala', they share as well.
The disagreement is not about the qiyas itself but about the ruling of the asl (lineage inheritance).
4
Understand the general rule on non-transferability of wala'
The passage concludes that wala' cannot be sold, gifted, or inherited because it is a bond (luhmah) like lineage (nasab), which is also non-transferable.
This rule is unanimous in the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the grandfather receives the entire estate of the freed slave. Under the Sahibayn, the estate is split between grandfather and brother, presumably equally.
The text does not specify the fraction, but the general principle is division between them.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance of wala' (clientage) when the freed slave dies without an inheritor within the lineage of slavery. The issue: if the deceased leaves a grandfather of the freed slave (i.e., the father of the freed slave's father who is also a freedman in the same wala' chain) and a brother of the freed slave (a full or consanguine brother who is also a freedman), who inherits? Abu Hanifa holds that the entire inheritance goes to the grandfather, analogizing to the rule in lineage inheritance that brothers are excluded (hajb hirman) by the grandfather. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the wealth is divided between the grandfather and the brother, because in lineage inheritance brothers share with the grandfather (according to their view in a well-known dispute: for Abu Hanifa, grandfather excludes brothers; for the Sahibayn, brothers share). The commentary clarifies that each position is based on analogy (qiyas) to the respective ruling on inheritance of lineage. The passage concludes with a general rule: wala' cannot be sold, gifted, or inherited, because it is a bond of relationship (luhmah) akin to lineage (nasab), and lineage itself is not transferable by sale or gift.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā'): Clientage; a legal bond established by manumission, giving the former master (or his heirs) a right of inheritance from the freed slave (and vice versa) under certain conditions.
لحمة (luḥmah): A bond of relationship; here, used to describe wala' as a connection similar to lineage (nasab), making it non-transferable.

Key Takeaway
The distribution of wala' inheritance follows the same rules as lineage inheritance, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute because the imams themselves differ on the lineage inheritance rule between grandfather and brother.

Determining the Base Number (Asl al-Mas'alah) for Inheritance Fractions
Matn and commentary on base number rules for inheritance fractions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the base number (asl al-mas'alah) for common inheritance scenarios involving halves, thirds, fourths, and eighths by applying the LCM method and the memorized rules from the matn.

Core Idea
The base number equals the LCM of the fixed share denominators, not their sum.

This lesson introduces the foundational step of any inheritance calculation: finding the base number (asl al-mas'alah). The matn from Al-Quduri, explained in Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, lists common patterns of fixed shares (furud) and their corresponding base numbers. Mastering this rule allows you to convert fractional shares into whole numbers for distribution among heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed shares present
Look at the inheritance case and list all Quranic fixed shares (e.g., 1/2, 1/4, 1/8, 1/3, 2/3). Ignore residuary heirs (asabah) for now.
Only the fixed shares determine the denominators.
2
List their denominators
Write down the denominators of each fixed share. For example, 1/2 gives denominator 2; 1/4 gives denominator 4.
Simplify fractions if needed.
3
Find the least common multiple (LCM)
Compute the LCM of the denominators. This is the base number.
For 2 and 4, LCM is 4; for 2 and 8, LCM is 8.
4
Match the pattern to the matn
Verify that the pattern matches one of the memorized rules: two halves -> base 2; half and residue -> base 2; third and residue -> base 3; etc.
The matn covers only combinations with denominators 2, 3, 4, and 8.
5
Apply to an example
Take a concrete case, such as husband (1/4) and daughter (1/2). Denominators: 4 and 2. LCM = 4. Base is 4. Then assign shares: husband = 1/4 = 1 part; daughter = 1/2 = 2 parts; residue (if any heir) = 1 part.
Always check if the total of fixed shares exceeds the base, requiring 'awl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حساب الفرائض
اثنين .
حساب الفرائض
إذا كان في المسألة نصف ونصف، أو نصف وما بقي : فأصلها من
اثنين .
وإن كان ثلث وما بقي، أو ثلثان وما بقي : فأصلها من ثلاثة . وإن كان ربع وما بقي، أو ربع ونصف : فأصلها من أربعة . وإن كان ثمن وما بقي : أو ثمن ونصف وما بقي : فأصلها من ثمانية .
قال
حساب الفرائض
رحمه الله : (إذا كان في المسألة نصف ونصف، أو نصف وما بقي: فأصلها من اثنين، فالأول كزوج وأخت لأب وأم، أو لأب،
والثاني: كزوج وعم.
* قوله: (وإن كان فيها ثلث وما بقي، أو ثلثان وما بقي: فأصلها من
ثلاثة)، فالأول : كأم وعم، والثاني: كابنتين وعم.

Calculation of Inheritances. Two. Calculation of Inheritances. If in the case there is a half and a half, or a half and what remains: its base is from two. And if there is a third and what remains, or two-thirds and what remains: its base is from three. And if there is a fourth and what remains, or a fourth and a half: its base is from four. And if there is an eighth and what remains, or an eighth and a half and what remains: its base is from eight. He said - may Allah have mercy on him - (If in the case there is a half and a half, or a half and what remains: its base is from two. The first is like a husband and a full or consanguine sister, and the second: like a husband and a paternal uncle). His saying: (And if in it there is a third and what remains, or two-thirds and what remains: its base is from three; the first like a mother and a paternal uncle, the second like two daughters and a paternal uncle). His saying: (And if in it there is a fourth and what remains, or a fourth and a half: its base is from four; the first like a wife and an asabah, the second like a husband and a daughter). His saying: (And if in it there is an eighth and what remains, or an eighth and a half and what remains: its base is from eight; the first like a wife and a son, the second like a wife and a daughter).
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri (matn) with commentary from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira states the fundamental rule for determining the base number (asl al-mas'alah) in inheritance calculations. The base number is the least common multiple of the denominators of the Quranic shares (fard) present. The matn lists the common cases: (1) If there are two halves, or a half and a residue, the base is 2. Example: husband (1/2) and full sister (1/2) -> base 2. Or husband (1/2) and uncle (asabah -> takes residue) -> base 2. (2) If there is a third and residue, or two-thirds and residue, the base is 3. Example: mother (1/3) and uncle (asabah) -> base 3. Or two daughters (2/3) and uncle (asabah) -> base 3. (3) If there is a fourth and residue, or a fourth and a half, base is 4. Example: wife (1/4) and son (asabah) -> base 4. Or husband (1/4) and daughter (1/2) -> base 4 (since 1/2=2/4, total 3/4, residue 1/4 to asabah). (4) If there is an eighth and residue, or an eighth and a half and residue, base is 8. Example: wife (1/8) and son (asabah) -> base 8. Or wife (1/8) and daughter (1/2=4/8) -> total 5/8, residue 3/8 to asabah. The commentary provides examples for each case. Note: When fractions like half and fourth appear together, the base is the LCM of 2 and 4, which is 4, so the rule is covered under 'fourth and half'.

Key Terms
أصل المسألة (aṣl al-mas'alah): The base number from which shares are derived; the least common multiple of the denominators of the fixed shares (furud) present.
نصف (niṣf): One-half (1/2), a fixed Quranic share for certain heirs like husband, daughter, sister, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an inheritance case involves a husband (1/4) and a daughter (1/2), what is the base number (asl al-mas'alah)?
A) 2
B) 4
C) 8
D) 6

Key Takeaway
Always compute the base number as the LCM of the fixed share denominators; memorise the common patterns from the matn for speed.
