Faraid — Inheritance
beginner
10 min
Determining the Base Number (Asl al-Mas'alah) in Inheritance
Rules for base numbers in inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the base number for common fraction combinations (halves, thirds, quarters, eighths, sixths, and their combinations) and apply the rule to simple inheritance cases.

Core Idea
The base number is the least common multiple of the share denominators.

Inheritance calculations begin by determining the base number (asl al-mas'alah), the smallest number from which all fractional shares can be proportioned. The Hanafi tradition provides clear rules for standard combinations of shares. This lesson covers the base numbers for halves, thirds, quarters, eighths, and sixths, along with their combinations, as presented in al-Jawharah al-Nayirah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fractional shares present
List all heirs and their prescribed Quranic shares. For example, a husband gets 1/2, a sister gets 1/2, or a mother gets 1/3, etc.
If a share is 'remainder' (asabah), treat it as unspecified; the base is determined by the fixed shares.
2
Find the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators
The base number is the LCM of all denominators of the fixed shares. For example, denominators 2 and 2 give LCM 2; denominators 2 and 3 give LCM 6.
If there is only one fixed share and the rest are asabah, the base is the denominator of that share.
3
Match with the classical rules in the text
The author gives memorization anchors: two halves → base 2; half and remainder → base 2; third and remainder → base 3; etc. These follow from the LCM principle.
These rules cover the most common cases and help quick computation.
4
Apply the base to distribute shares
Convert each share to an equivalent fraction with the base as denominator. For example, 1/2 with base 2 becomes 1/2; 1/2 and 1/2 with base 2 become 1/2+1/2 = 1 (no remainder). If using asabah, the remainder after fixed shares goes to the residuary heir.
If the sum of fractions exceeds 1, the case requires 'awl, which is covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حساب الفرائض
إذا كان في المسألة نصف ونصف، أو نصف وما بقي: فأصلها من اثنين. وإن كان ثلث وما بقي، أو ثلثان وما بقي: فأصلها من ثلاثة. وإن كان ربع وما بقي، أو ربع ونصف: فأصلها من أربعة. وإن كان ثمن وما بقي: أو ثمن ونصف وما بقي: فأصلها من ثمانية.
قال رحمه الله: (إذا كان في المسألة نصف ونصف، أو نصف وما بقي: فأصلها من اثنين، فالأول كزوج وأخت لأب وأم، أو لأب، والثاني: كزوج وعم.
* قوله: (وإن كان فيها ثلث وما بقي، أو ثلثان وما بقي: فأصلها من ثلاثة)، فالأول: كأم وعم، والثاني: كابنتين وعم.
قوله: (وإن كان فيها ربع وما بقي أو ربع ونصف: فأصلها من أربعة)، فالأول: كزوجة وعصبة، والثاني: كزوج وبنت.
قوله: (وإن كان فيها تُمُنٌ وما بقي، أو تُمن ونصف وما بقي: فأصلها من ثمانية، فالأول كزوجة وابن، والثاني: كزوجة وبنت.
وإذا كان سدس وما بقي، أو نصفٌ وثلث، أو سدس: فأصلها من ستة، وتَعُول إلى سبعة وثمانية، وتسعة، وعشرة.
وإن كان مع الربع ثلث، أو سدس: فأصلها من اثني عشر، وتعولُ إلى ثلاثة عشر، وخمسة عشر، وسبعة عشر.
قوله: (وإن كان فيها نصف وثلث، أو نصف وسدس: فأصلها من ستة)، فالأول: كأم وأخت لأب وأم، أو لأب، والثاني: كأم وبنت. قوله: (وتعول إلى سبعة وثمانية، وتسعة وعشرة).
فالأول: كزوج وأختين لأبوين، أو لأب: فهذه تعول إلى سبعة. والثاني: كزوج وأختين لأب وأم وأخ لأم: فهذه تعول إلى ثمانية. والثالث: كزوج وأختين لأب وأم وأخوين لأم: فهذه تعول إلى تسعة. والرابع: كما لو كان مع هؤلاء أم: فهي تعول إلى عشرة.
* قوله: (ولا تعول إلى غير ذلك(۱)).
العول: هو الزيادة في الفرائض عند تضايق المستحقين.
*
قوله: (وإن كان مع الربع ثلث أو سدس: فأصلها من اثني عشر)،
فالأول: كزوجة وأم، والثاني: كزوجة وأخت لأم.
* قوله: (وتعول إلى ثلاثة عشر وخمسة عشر، وسبعة عشر).
فالتي تعول إلى ثلاثة عشر: كزوج وأم وابنتان.
(۱) هذه المقولة غير مثبتة في نسخ القدوري.
Inheritance calculation. If the case contains a half and a half, or a half and the remainder: its base is from two. If it contains a third and the remainder, or two-thirds and the remainder: its base is from three. If it contains a quarter and the remainder, or a quarter and a half: its base is from four. If it contains an eighth and the remainder, or an eighth and a half and the remainder: its base is from eight. He (the author) said: (If the case contains a half and a half, or a half and the remainder: its base is from two). The first is like a husband and a sister from father and mother, or from father; the second: like a husband and a paternal uncle. His statement: (If it contains a third and the remainder, or two-thirds and the remainder: its base is from three). The first: like a mother and a paternal uncle; the second: like two daughters and a paternal uncle. His statement: (If it contains a quarter and the remainder, or a quarter and a half: its base is from four). The first: like a wife and an asabah; the second: like a husband and a daughter. His statement: (If it contains an eighth and the remainder, or an eighth and a half and the remainder: its base is from eight). The first: like a wife and a son; the second: like a wife and a daughter. And if it contains a sixth and the remainder, or a half and a third, or a sixth: its base is from six, and it increases (ta'ulu) to seven, eight, nine, and ten. And if with the quarter there is a third or a sixth: its base is from twelve, and it increases to thirteen, fifteen, and seventeen. His statement: (If it contains a half and a third, or a half and a sixth: its base is from six). The first: like a mother and a sister from father and mother, or from father; the second: like a mother and a daughter. His statement: (And it increases to seven, eight, nine, and ten). The first: like a husband and two full sisters, or from father: this increases to seven. The second: like a husband and two full sisters and a maternal brother: this increases to eight. The third: like a husband and two full sisters and two maternal brothers: this increases to nine. The fourth: as if with these there is a mother: it increases to ten. * His statement: (And it does not increase to other than that). al-'awl is the increase in the shares when the entitled ones are constrained. * His statement: (And if with the quarter there is a third or a sixth: its base is from twelve). The first: like a wife and a mother; the second: like a wife and a maternal sister. * His statement: (And it increases to thirteen, fifteen, and seventeen). That which increases to thirteen: like a husband, a mother, and two daughters. (1) This statement is not verified in the copies of al-Quduri.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah explains the rules for determining the base number (asl al-mas'alah) in inheritance calculations, a foundational step for distributing the estate. The base number is the least common denominator of the fractional shares present. The author gives the base numbers for common combinations: if half and half or half and remainder, base 2; if third and remainder or two-thirds and remainder, base 3; if quarter and remainder or quarter and half, base 4; if eighth and remainder or eighth and half and remainder, base 8; if sixth and remainder, half and third, or half and sixth, base 6; if quarter with third or sixth, base 12. The text also introduces the concept of 'awl (increase), which occurs when the sum of the prescribed shares exceeds the base, requiring proportional reduction. Examples are provided for each case. For instance, a husband (1/2) and two full sisters (2/3) yield base 6, but total shares are 7/6, so the base increases to 7 ('awl to 7). The note at the end clarifies that 'awl only occurs to these specific numbers (7,8,9,10 for base 6; 13,15,17 for base 12). The comment about 'ولا تعول إلى غير ذلك' is a gloss not found in older copies of al-Quduri. The section ends with a definition of 'awl as the increase in shares when the heirs are many.

Key Terms
أصل المسألة (asl al-mas'alah): The base number from which shares are proportioned in inheritance (e.g., 6 for halves and thirds).
العول (al-'awl): The increase of the base number when the sum of shares exceeds the original base.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the base number for a case containing a mother (1/3) and a paternal uncle (asabah)?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 6
D) 12

Key Takeaway
The base number is the LCM of the denominators of the fixed shares; simple combinations have set bases as per the classical rules.

Faraid — Inheritance
intermediate
10 min
Understanding 'Awl: When Shares Exceed the Base
Concept of 'awl and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when 'awl is necessary, apply the increase to the base number, and calculate the reduced shares for the heirs.

Core Idea
When the sum of prescribed shares exceeds the base, the base is increased ('awl) to accommodate all shares proportionally.

Sometimes the fixed shares of the heirs add up to more than the whole estate. In such cases, the base number is increased to a larger value so that each heir receives a proportionally reduced share of the actual estate. This is called 'awl (increase). The Hanafi school accepts 'awl and has established the specific new bases for common combinations. This lesson examines the examples given in the text, particularly from base 6 and base 12.

Guided Path
1
List all fixed shares and find their sum
Write the shares as fractions with a common denominator (the initial base) and add them. If the sum is greater than 1, 'awl is needed.
The initial base is the LCM of the denominators.
2
Increase the base to the sum of the numerators
The new base becomes the total of the numerators. For example, if initial base is 6 and numerators sum to 7, the new base is 7.
This ensures that each share becomes a fraction of the new base, and the sum equals 1 (i.e., the whole estate).
3
Recalculate each heir's share as a fraction of the new base
Each heir's share remains the same numerator, but the denominator becomes the new base. For example, a 1/2 share becomes 3/7 instead of 3/6.
The actual fractional portions of the estate are reduced, but everyone receives their entitlement proportionally.
4
Verify that the sum of new shares equals 1
Add the new fractions; they should add up to exactly 1 (e.g., 3/7 + 4/7 = 1).
The increase in the base is called 'awl, and the new base is one of the set numbers (7,8,9,10 for base 6; 13,15,17 for base 12).

Source Text

Faraid — Inheritance
beginner
6 min
Base Numbers 6 and 12 with 'Awl to Various Sums
Faraid rule: base numbers 6 and 12 with 'awl outcomes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the inheritance base number (asl al-mas'alah) for combinations involving half, third, sixth, and quarter, and apply 'awl to distribute the estate when shares exceed the base.

Core Idea
The base number for inheritance fractions is either 6 or 12; 'awl increases the denominator to accommodate excess shares.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the foundational base numbers for common combinations of prescribed shares in Hanafi inheritance law. Understanding when the base is 6 or 12, and how 'awl adjusts the distribution, is essential for accurate estate division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the combination of prescribed shares
Look at the shares present: half (1/2), third (1/3), sixth (1/6), quarter (1/4). The combination determines the base number. Combinations with half and third, half and sixth, or sixth with remainder use base 6. Combinations with quarter and third, or quarter and sixth, use base 12.
If there is a half and a third, the base is 6. If there is a quarter and a third, the base is 12.
2
Determine the initial base number (asl al-mas'alah)
For half+third/half+sixth/sixth+remainder: base = 6. For quarter+third/quarter+sixth: base = 12. This is the least common multiple of the share denominators.
For half (denominator 2) and third (3), LCM is 6. For quarter (4) and third (3), LCM is 12.
3
Convert shares to fractions of the base
Express each heir's share as a fraction of the base number. For example, half = 3/6, third = 2/6, sixth = 1/6, quarter = 3/12. Sum these fractions to check if they exceed the base.
If the sum is less than or equal to 1, no 'awl is needed. If greater, 'awl is required.
4
Apply 'awl if the sum exceeds the base
When the sum of shares exceeds the base, increase the base number to the sum. All shares are then expressed as fractions of the new base, reducing proportionally. The text lists possible 'awl sums: for base 6, to 7,8,9,10; for base 12, to 13,15,17.
'Awl does not reduce any heir's share absolutely; it adjusts the denominator so each gets a proportional fraction of the estate.
5
Verify with an example
Take the example of husband (1/4), mother (1/6), two daughters (2/3). Base = 12; shares: 3/12 + 2/12 + 8/12 = 13/12 >1, so base 'awls to 13. Final shares: husband 3/13, mother 2/13, each daughter 4/13.
The total estate is divided into 13 equal parts. This is the 'awl to 13 case.

And if with the quarter there is a third or a sixth: then its base is from twelve, and it 'awls to thirteen, fifteen, and seventeen. Commentary
This passage is a faraid (inheritance calculation) passage. It first states the general rule: when the shares include half and third, or half and sixth, or sixth with remainder, the base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 6. This base can be increased through 'awl to 7, 8, 9, or 10, depending on the combination of shares. The text then provides examples: a husband with two sisters (from both parents or from father) gives base 6 and 'awls to 7; if there is also a maternal brother, it 'awls to 8; with two maternal brothers, to 9; and if a mother is added, to 10. Next, the rule for quarter with third or sixth: the base is 12, which can 'awl to 13, 15, or 17. Examples: a wife and mother give base 12 and 'awl to 13 (since wife gets 1/4=3, mother 1/3=4, total 7, but actually the text says 'awl to 13? Wait: base 12, shares: wife 3, mother 4, remainder 5 to daughter? The text says 'awl to 13: husband, mother, two daughters: husband 1/4=3, mother 1/6=2, two daughters 2/3=8, total 13). The passage concludes with a definition: 'awl is the increase in the base denominator when the shares of the entitled heirs exceed the original base. The Hanafi school follows this method of proportional reduction.

Key Terms
العول (al-'awl): Increase in the base denominator of the inheritance estate when the sum of prescribed shares exceeds the original base.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the base number and its 'awl when a husband, mother, and two daughters are the heirs?
A) Base 12, 'awl to 13
B) Base 6, 'awl to 7
C) Base 24, 'awl to 27
D) Base 12, 'awl to 15

Key Takeaway
The base number for inheritance fractions with half or quarter combinations is 6 or 12; 'awl proportionally increases the denominator when shares exceed the estate.

The Minbariyyah Case: Base 24 and 'Awl to 27
Explains the base 24 for eighth and thirds, 'awl to 27, and the correction method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate inheritance shares in a case where the base 24 'awls to 27 (e.g., the minbariyyah case) and apply the correction method (tashih) when a group's shares are not evenly divisible.

Core Idea
The minbariyyah case shows how an eighth becomes a ninth when shares 'awl to 27.

This lesson focuses on a classic inheritance problem involving a wife, two daughters, and both parents. The prescribed shares exceed the base 24, requiring an 'awl increase to 27. You will learn the calculation step by step and understand why this case is historically significant, as well as the method for correcting the base when shares of multiple heirs (e.g., sisters) do not divide evenly among them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and their prescribed shares
List all heirs: wife (1/8), two daughters (2/3), father (1/6), mother (1/6). Note that the presence of children gives parents 1/6 each and reduces wife to 1/8.
The two daughters together get 2/3; individually each gets 1/3 of the estate before 'awl.
2
Determine the least common multiple (LCM)
Find the LCM of the denominators: 8, 3, 6, 6. The LCM is 24. This becomes the base (asl al-masalah).
Choose 24 because it is the smallest number divisible by all denominators.
3
Convert each share to the base
Convert fractions to parts of 24: wife 1/8 = 3/24, two daughters 2/3 = 16/24, father 1/6 = 4/24, mother 1/6 = 4/24.
The sum is 3+16+4+4 = 27, which exceeds the base 24.
4
Apply 'awl to increase the base
Since total shares (27) are greater than the base (24), the base is increased to 27. Each share retains its numerator while the denominator changes to 27.
This is called 'awl (increase). The wife's share becomes 3/27 = 1/9 – hence 'its eighth returned to a ninth.'
5
Apply correction if needed (tashih)
If a group of heirs (e.g., multiple sisters) had shares that do not divide evenly among them, multiply the base (or 'awl base) by the number of individuals in that group to obtain a new corrected base. In this case, the two daughters' share of 16 is divisible by 2, so no correction is needed.
For example, if there were three daughters, 16 is not divisible by 3, so you would multiply 27 by 3, giving a new base of 81, and each daughter would get 16/81.

And if the portions of a group do not divide upon them, then multiply their number by the base of the case, and its 'awl if it is an 'awl case, and what comes out: then from it the case is corrected. Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage continues the faraid rules. It covers cases where the eighth (1/8) appears alongside two-thirds (2/3) or a sixth (1/6). The base number for such cases is 24. For example, a wife (1/8 = 3/24), parents (each 1/6 = 4/24, total 8/24), and a son (residue 13/24) total 24 exactly — no 'awl. But when the wife has two daughters (2/3 = 16/24) and parents (each 1/6 = 4/24, total 8/24), total 27/24, so base 'awls to 27. This specific case is famously known as 'al-minbariyyah' because Ali (RA) gave this ruling while on the pulpit, stating 'its eighth returned to a ninth' (meaning the wife's share 1/8 becomes 3/27 = 1/9 after 'awl). The passage then explains the method for correcting the denominator when the shares of a group of heirs (e.g., multiple sisters) do not divide evenly among them: multiply the number of individuals in that group by the base (or the 'awl base) to get a new total from which the shares are computed. This ensures each individual gets an integer share.

Key Terms
المنبرية (al-minbariyyah): A specific inheritance case with wife, two daughters, and parents, where the base 'awls from 24 to 27, and the wife's share becomes 1/9 instead of 1/8.
العول (al-'awl): Increasing the base denominator of an inheritance case when the sum of fractional shares exceeds the original base.
التصحيح (al-tashih): Multiplying the base (or 'awl base) by the number of individuals in a group to ensure integer shares for each heir.

Faraid Adjustment: 'Awl and Tashih – From Minbariyyah to the General Rule
Explaining faraid rules for base adjustment, 'awl, and tashih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the 'awl process for the Minbariyyah case and apply the tashih rule when shares do not divide evenly among heirs.

Core Idea
The Hanafi method for inheritance distribution uses 'awl to adjust the base when shares exceed the estate, and tashih to ensure each heir receives an integer number of shares.

In this passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah, we learn the arithmetic rules for adjusting the base number (asl) of an estate when the prescribed shares exceed the total, a process called 'awl. We also see how to correct the base when shares cannot be evenly divided among heirs of the same category, a process called tashih. These rules are essential for just distribution of inheritance according to Hanafi faraid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific 'awl case
Locate the case where the estate includes an eighth (wife) along with two-thirds (two daughters) and both parents. The original base is 24, but after 'awl it becomes 27. This case is called al-Minbariyyah.
Remember: the shares are wife 3, daughters 16 (8 each), father 4, mother 4 out of 27.
2
Understand the evidence for 'awl
The passage narrates that 'Ali gave this ruling on the minbar, saying 'Its eighth became a ninth.' This indicates the shares were reduced proportionally. Hanafis accept 'awl based on the practice of the Companions, considered ijma'.
The Companions did not object, establishing 'awl as a valid principle.
3
Learn the general tashih rule
When shares of a group of heirs do not divide evenly among their number, multiply the number of individuals in that group by the original base (including any 'awl) to get the new base. If the shares and the number have a common factor, multiply only the wafq (the number after dividing by the common factor).
Example with no common factor: wife and two brothers. Wife gets 1 share, brothers get 3 shares. 3 does not divide by 2, so multiply 2 by base 4 = 8.
4
Apply the wafq subcase
When the shares and the number of heirs have a common factor, use the wafq. Example: wife and six brothers. Wife gets 1 share, brothers get 3 shares. 3 and 6 share a factor of 3, so wafq of 6 is 2. Multiply 2 by base 4 = 8. Wife gets 2, each brother gets 1.
This simplifies the arithmetic to avoid fractions.

Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah explains key principles for calculating inheritance shares (faraid). It begins by stating a specific case: when the estate includes an eighth (thumn) along with either two-thirds (sulusan) or one-sixth (sudus), the base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 24. This base can increase ('awl) to 27. The commentator gives examples: for a wife (1/8), parents (each 1/6), and a son (residue), the base is 24; for a wife and two daughters (2/3), the base is also 24. The case that 'awls to 27 is a wife, two daughters, and both parents. This is known as al-Minbariyyah because 'Ali (may Allah honor him) gave this ruling while on the pulpit, noting that the eighth (wife's share) became a ninth (out of 27). The story is narrated by Abu 'Ubayd and others.

The passage then addresses the general rule for tashih (correcting the base) when shares do not divide evenly among heirs. If the shares of a group of heirs do not divide evenly among them, multiply the number of individuals in that group by the original base (including any 'awl) to get a new base from which the distribution works. Two subcases: if the shares and the number of heirs have no common factor (tabayun), multiply the entire number; if they have a common factor (tawafuq), multiply only the wafq (the reduced number after dividing by the common factor). Examples: a wife and two brothers: wife's share 1/4 (1 share), brothers get 3 shares; 3 does not divide by 2, so multiply 2 (number of brothers) by the base 4 = 8, wife gets 2, each brother gets 3. For a wife and six brothers: wife gets 1 share, brothers get 3 shares; 3 and 6 have a common factor of 3, so wafq of 6 is 2, multiply 2 by base 4 = 8, wife gets 2, each brother gets 1 (since 3 shares ÷ 6 brothers = 0.5? Actually each gets 0.5? But the example says from 8, wife takes 2, brothers take 6, each brother 1? The text says 'from it the problem is valid' but does not specify each brother's share. In fact, 6 brothers sharing 6 shares each get 1 share. So correct: wife 2, each brother 1. This illustrates the rule.

Key Terms
المنبرية (al-Minbariyyah): A specific faraid case that 'awls to 27, named after 'Ali's ruling on the minbar.
عول ('awl): Increase in the base number (asl) of the estate when total shares exceed the base, causing proportional reduction of each share.
وفق (wafq): The reduced number after dividing the number of heirs and their shares by their common factor (e.g., 6 and 3 -> wafq of 6 is 2).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The principle of 'awl adjusts the base proportionally, and tashih corrects the base when shares do not divide evenly among heirs.

Faraid — Inheritance

Tashih al-Mas'alah: Adjusting the Inheritance Base
Methods of tashih when shares do not divide evenly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of tashih to adjust the inheritance base when shares do not divide evenly among heirs, distinguishing between tabayun and muwafaqah.

Core Idea
When shares don't divide evenly, multiply the number of heirs (or their agreement factor) by the base to get a new whole base.

Inheritance calculations often result in fractional shares that must be adjusted so each heir receives a whole number. The Hanafi school provides a systematic method called tashih (adjustment) to increase the base number proportionally. This lesson covers the two key scenarios: when shares and heir count have no common factor (tabayun) and when they share a factor (muwafaqah), as well as handling multiple groups of heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups whose shares break
Look at the heirs whose allotted shares cannot be divided evenly among their number. These are the groups requiring adjustment. For a single group, note the relationship between the shares and the number of heirs.
2
Determine tabayun or muwafaqah
Check if the number of shares and the number of heirs have a common factor greater than one. If they have no common factor (tabayun), you will multiply the full number of heirs. If they share a factor (muwafaqah), reduce the number by dividing by that factor (take the wafq) and multiply by that reduced number.
Example: 3 shares for 6 heirs → common factor 3 → reduce 6 to 2.
3
Apply the adjustment for one group
Multiply the number (or wafq) by the original base. The result is the new base from which all shares are recalculated.
Wife (1/4) and 2 brothers: base 4, brothers' shares 3, number 2 (tabayun) → multiply 2 × 4 = 8.
4
Handle multiple groups
If shares break on two or more groups, multiply the numbers (or wafq) of each group together sequentially, then multiply the result by the original base. This yields the final adjusted base.
Example: wives (2), grandmothers (5), brothers (3): 2 × 5 × 3 = 30; then 30 × 12 = 360.
5
Distribute the new shares
Each heir's original share count is multiplied by the multiplier (the product of group numbers/wafq) to get their new share count. Verify the sum equals the new base.

Like two wives, five grandmothers, three maternal brothers, and a paternal uncle: Its origin is from twelve: For the two wives: one-fourth, three; for the grandmothers: one-sixth, two shares; for the maternal brothers: one-third, four; and for the paternal uncle: what remains, which is three. And it breaks on the two wives, the grandmothers, and the brothers. So multiply the number of wives, which is two, by the number of grandmothers: it becomes ten; then multiply the ten by three, the number of brothers: it becomes thirty; then multiply the thirty by the base of the case, which is twelve: it becomes three hundred and sixty, and from it the case is valid. Then we say: Whoever has something in the estate is multiplied by thirty: For the two wives: three times thirty is ninety, which is one-fourth of the total; each one gets forty-five. For the grandmothers: two shares times thirty is sixty; each one gets twelve. For the brothers: four times thirty is one hundred and twenty; each one gets forty. And for the uncle: three times thirty is ninety. That totals three hundred and sixty.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains the rules for adjusting the base number (asl al-mas'alah) in inheritance calculations when the shares of heirs do not divide evenly among them. The author first addresses cases where shares break on a single group of heirs. He gives an example of a wife and two brothers: the wife gets 1/4 (1 share), the brothers get the remaining 3/4 (3 shares). Since 3 shares cannot be divided equally between two brothers (tabayun), we multiply the number of brothers (2) by the original base (4) to get a new base of 8. The shares then become: wife 2, each brother 3 (total 6). The same principle applies when there is agreement (muwafaqah) between the shares and the number of heirs, as in the example of a wife and six brothers: the brothers' 3 shares and their number 6 share a common factor of 3, so we take the agreement (2, which is 6/3) and multiply by 4 to get 8. Then the author notes the case of 'awl (increase of base) using the example of a husband and three sisters: the base shifts from 6 to 7, and after further adjustment (tashih) becomes 21. On page 470, the rule extends to cases with multiple groups of heirs whose shares break. The method is to multiply the numbers of each group together in sequence, then multiply the result by the original base. A comprehensive example is given: two wives, five grandmothers, three maternal brothers, and a paternal uncle. Their shares from base 12 are 3, 2, 4, and 3 respectively. Each group's number (2, 5, 3) has no common factor with its share, so we multiply 2×5=10, then 10×3=30, then 30×12=360. The final distribution is calculated by multiplying each group's original shares by 30. This method ensures that all heirs receive whole shares without fractions.

Key Terms
أصل المسألة (asl al-mas'alah): The base number from which shares are calculated (e.g., 4 for a wife).
تباين (tabayun): When the shares and number of heirs have no common factor (e.g., 3 shares for 2 heirs).
موافقة (muwafaqah): When shares and number of heirs share a common factor, so one multiplies the agreement part (e.g., 3 shares and 6 heirs agree by 3, so multiply 2).
عول (‘awl): When the sum of prescribed shares exceeds the base, causing the base to increase proportionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a woman and six brothers, what is the adjusted base number after tashih?
A) 4
B) 6
C) 8
D) 12

Key Takeaway
Tashih ensures that all heirs receive whole shares by adjusting the base number proportionally when shares do not divide evenly.

Adjusting the Base Number in Faraid: The Three Rules of Tashih
Explains the arithmetic rules for tashih when shares break on multiple groups

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of tashih (tamathul, tadakhul, and tawafuq) to correctly adjust the base number when shares of multiple groups of heirs do not divide evenly.

Core Idea
Adjust the base by multiplying group numbers, but first check for equality, inclusion, or common factor.

In Hanafi inheritance calculations, when the fractional shares of a group of heirs cannot be divided evenly among its individual members, we must adjust the base number (asl al-mas'alah) through a process called tashih. This lesson focuses on the arithmetic rules when shares break on more than one group. The key is to first compare the numbers of individuals in each group—they may be equal (tamathul), one may be a divisor of the other (tadakhul), or they may share a common factor (tawafuq). Each relationship dictates a specific multiplication, saving unnecessary computation. Understanding these rules ensures that every heir receives a whole number of shares.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups where shares break
Look at each group of heirs with the same share type. If the fractional share (e.g., 1/4, 1/6) cannot be divided evenly by the number of individuals in that group, that group is 'breaking.' List the number of individuals in each such group.
For example, if two wives have a 1/4 share (3/12), 3 does not divide evenly by 2, so the wives group breaks.
2
Record the number of individuals in each group
Note the count (e.g., 2 wives, 5 grandmothers, 3 uterine brothers). These numbers will be used in the multiplication.
3
Compare the numbers: check for tamathul, tadakhul, or tawafuq
For every pair of groups, determine the relationship: tamathul (equal numbers), tadakhul (one divides the other), or tawafuq (neither, but share a common factor). This comparison is done sequentially across all groups.
If there are more than two groups, you process them pairwise: first two, then their result with the third, etc.
4
Apply the corresponding rule to compute the multiplier
For tamathul: use one of the numbers. For tadakhul: use the larger number. For tawafuq: find the common factor, divide one number by it (wafq), then multiply by the other number. Multiply the results of pairwise comparisons to get the final multiplier.
The multiplier is the number by which you will multiply the original base.
5
Multiply the multiplier by the original base to get the adjusted base
Take the multiplier from step 4 and multiply it by the original base number (asl al-mas'alah). The result is the new base from which all heirs will receive whole shares.
Then assign shares by multiplying each heir's original share by the multiplier and dividing by the group size.

فإن تساوت الأعداد : أجزأ أحدهما عن الآخر، كامرأتين، وأخوين، فاضرب الاثنين في أصل المسألة.

And if the shares of two groups or more do not divide evenly, then multiply one of the groups by the other, then what results by the third group, then what results by the base of the problem. For the wife: one-fourth, two shares, and for the brothers: six, each one share. His saying: (And if the shares of two groups or more do not divide evenly, then multiply one of the groups by the other, then what results by the third group, then what results by the base of the problem). For example, two wives, five grandmothers, three uterine brothers, and a paternal uncle: its base is from twelve: for the two wives: one-fourth, three; for the grandmothers: one-sixth, two shares; for the uterine brothers: one-third, four; and for the uncle: what remains, three. And it broke over the wives, grandmothers, and brothers. So multiply the number of wives, two, by the number of grandmothers: yields ten; then multiply ten by three, the number of brothers: yields thirty; then multiply thirty by the base of the problem, twelve: yields three hundred sixty, and from it it becomes correct. Then we say: whoever has a share in the inheritance multiplied by thirty: for the two wives: three times thirty yields ninety, which is one-fourth of the total, each one forty-five; for the grandmothers: two shares times thirty yields sixty, each one twelve; for the brothers: four times thirty yields one hundred twenty, each one forty; for the uncle: three times thirty yields ninety; all that is three hundred sixty. So if the numbers are equal, one suffices for the other, like two women and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the problem. If one of the numbers is a part of the other, the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers: if you multiply four, it suffices for the two brothers. And if one number coincides with the other (i.e., shares a common factor), you multiply the concordant part (wafq) of one by the entirety of the other, then multiply what results by the base of the problem. * His saying: (If the numbers are equal, one suffices for the other, like two women and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the problem). This is called al-mutamathil. Its base is from four: for the two wives: one-fourths share breaking on them, and for the two brothers: what remains, three, also breaking. And one of the two numbers suffices you for the other, so multiply two by four becomes eight: for the two wives: two shares, and for the two brothers: six. His saying: (And if one of the numbers is a part of the other, the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers: if you multiply four, it suffices for the number of the brothers). This is called al-mutadakhil. We say: the base of the problem is from four: for the wives: one share breaking on them, and for the brothers: three also breaking. So suffice with multiplying four, because two is included in it (i.e., two divides four). So multiply four by four becomes sixteen: for the wives: four, for the brothers: twelve. His saying: (If one number coincides with the other, you multiply the concordant part of one by the entirety of the other, then multiply what results by the base of the problem).
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayirah explains the arithmetic rules for adjusting the base number (asl al-mas'alah) in inheritance calculations when the fractional shares of a group of heirs do not divide evenly among its members. This is called 'tashih' or 'tashih al-mas'alah'. The text presents three cases based on the relationship between two numbers representing the number of individuals in two different groups: tamathul (equality), tadakhul (one exactly divides the other), and tawafuq (neither equality nor exact division but sharing a common factor greater than one). 

The author first gives the general method for when shares break on two or more groups: multiply the numbers of individuals in each successive group, then multiply by the original base number. He illustrates with a complex example involving two wives, five grandmothers, three uterine brothers, and a paternal uncle. The original base is 12, but shares for wives (3), grandmothers (2), brothers (4) are not divisible by 2,5,3 respectively. By multiplying 2×5=10, then 10×3=30, then 30×12=360, the adjusted base becomes 360, and each heir gets a whole number. 

Then he introduces three special cases: 
1. Tamathul (equal numbers): When two groups have the same number of individuals, e.g., two wives and two brothers. Instead of multiplying the group numbers together, you only need to multiply one of them (e.g., 2) by the original base. The resulting base is 8. 
2. Tadakhul (one is a part of the other): When one number divides the other exactly, e.g., four wives and two brothers. Since 2 is a divisor of 4, you multiply the larger number (4) by the original base. The base becomes 16. 
3. Tawafuq (coincidence with a common factor): When the two numbers are neither equal nor one a multiple of the other but share a common factor greater than 1. For example, 4 and 6 share a factor of 2. You take the 'wafq' (the part after dividing by the common factor) of one number and multiply it by the other number, then multiply by the original base. 

These rules are essential for a Hanafi faraid practitioner to correctly compute whole shares for each heir without fractions.

Key Terms
المتماثل (al-mutamāthil): Two numbers that are exactly equal, like 2 and 2.
المتداخل (al-mutadākhil): One number is a part or divisor of the other, like 2 and 4.
الموافق (al-mutawāfiq): Two numbers that share a common factor greater than 1, like 4 and 6 (common factor 2).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a faraid problem, two breaking groups have numbers 4 and 6. Which tashih rule applies and what is the multiplier?
A) Tamathul; multiplier = 4
B) Tadakhul; multiplier = 6
C) Tawafuq; multiplier = (4/2)×6 = 12
D) Tawafuq; multiplier = 4×6 = 24

Key Takeaway
In tashih, compare group numbers first to apply the simplest multiplication: use one for tamathul, the larger for tadakhul, and the wafq for tawafuq.

Tamathul: Correcting the Base When Heir Group Sizes Are Equal
Explaining tamathul as the first case for correcting the base number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tamathul rule to correct the base number when the numbers of two groups of heirs are equal.

Core Idea
When two heir groups have equal numbers, one number suffices to correct the base—simply multiply by that number.

In inheritance calculations, when a share is distributed among multiple heirs and does not divide evenly, we must correct the base number. The simplest case is tamathul, where the numbers of two groups of heirs are identical. This lesson explains how to apply this rule using the example of two wives and two brothers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and their shares
List the heirs and their prescribed shares. In the tamathul example, the heirs are two wives (fard 1/4) and two brothers (asabah, taking the remaining 3/4).
2
Compute the base number (asl al-masalah)
The base number is the least common multiple of the share denominators. Since the wives get 1/4 and the brothers get the remainder (treated as 3/4), the base is 4.
The wives receive 1 part, the brothers receive 3 parts out of 4.
3
Check if shares are broken over the heirs
A share is broken if it does not divide evenly among the heirs of that group. Here, the wives' share (1) is broken over 2 wives, and the brothers' share (3) is broken over 2 brothers.
We need to correct the base so that each heir receives a whole number of parts.
4
Apply the tamathul rule
Since the numbers of wives and brothers are both 2 (equal), we multiply the base (4) by one of these numbers (2). This is because one number suffices for the other.
The rule states: 'If the numbers are equal, one suffices for the other.'
5
Compute the corrected base and final shares
Multiply the base (4) by 2 to get 8. Now the wives' share becomes 2 parts (each wife gets 1), and the brothers' share becomes 6 parts (each brother gets 3). The case is now corrected.
Corrected base: 8. Distribution: wives 2, brothers 6.

If the numbers are equal: one suffices for the other, like two wives and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the case. If one number is a part of the other: the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers, if you multiply four, it suffices for the brothers. If one number agrees (is compatible) with the other: multiply the half of one by the whole of the other, then what results [is multiplied] by the base of the case. * His statement: (If the numbers are equal one suffices for the other, like two wives and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the case). This is called mutamathil. Its base is four: for the two wives a quarter, a share broken over them, and for the two brothers the remainder which is three also broken, and one of the numbers suffices you from the other, so multiply two by four, it becomes eight: for the two wives two shares, and for the two brothers six. His statement: (If one number is a part of the other: the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers: if you multiply four, it suffices from the number of brothers). This is called mutadakhil, so we say: the base of the case is four: for the wives a share broken over them and for the brothers three broken as well, so you dispense with multiplying four because the two are included in it, so multiply four by four: it becomes sixteen: for the wives four, and for the brothers twelve. His statement: (If one number agrees with the other: multiply the half of one by the whole of the other, then what results multiply by the base of the case. Like four women, a sister, and six uncles: the six agrees with the four by half, so multiply half of one by the whole of the other, then what results [by] the base of the case: it becomes forty-eight, and from it [the case] is corrected.)
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining three mathematical rules for correcting the base number (tas'hih al-mas'alah) when the shares of multiple heirs do not divide evenly. These rules apply when there are multiple heirs in the same category (e.g., multiple wives or multiple brothers) and the number of heirs does not divide evenly into the share. The commentator expands on each case with examples. First, tamathul (equality): when the numbers of two groups of heirs are equal, such as two wives and two brothers, one number suffices for the other; the base is multiplied by that number. Second, tadakhul (inclusion): when one number is a multiple of the other, like four wives and two brothers, the larger number suffices, so multiply the base by four. Third, tawafuq (agreement): when the numbers share a common factor, like four wives and six uncles, use the reciprocal of one number multiplied by the other. All examples follow the standard Hanafi method of inheritance calculation.

Key Terms
المتماثل (al-mutamāthil): Two numbers that are equal, used when the number of heirs in two groups is identical.
المتداخل (al-mutadākhil): One number is a part (divisor) of the other, so the larger suffices for correction.
المتوافق (al-mutawāfiq): Two numbers share a common divisor greater than 1, requiring the use of the reciprocal.

Tadakhul: Correcting the Base When One Heir Group Size Is a Multiple of Another
Explaining tadakhul as the second case for correcting the base number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tadakhul rule to correct the base number when the number of heirs in one group is a divisor of the number in another.

Core Idea
When one heir group size is a multiple of the other, use the larger number to correct the base—the smaller is included in the larger.

In inheritance calculations, tadakhul applies when the number of heirs in one group is a divisor of the number in another. The larger number suffices for correction, as the smaller is already embedded in it. This lesson illustrates tadakhul with the example of four wives and two brothers.

Guided Path
1
List the heirs and their shares
The heirs are four wives (fard 1/4) and two brothers (asabah, taking the remaining 3/4).
2
Compute the base number
Base number is 4: wives get 1 part, brothers get 3 parts.
3
Identify the numbers of heirs in each broken share
Wives' share (1) is broken over 4 wives; brothers' share (3) is broken over 2 brothers. The numbers are 4 and 2.
4
Check if one number is a divisor of the other
2 is a divisor of 4 (2 is half of 4). Thus, tadakhul applies: the larger number (4) suffices for correction.
The smaller number is 'included' in the larger.
5
Apply tadakhul: multiply base by the larger number
Multiply the base (4) by 4 to get 16. Now wives' share becomes 4 (each wife gets 1), and brothers' share becomes 12 (each brother gets 6).
Corrected base: 16. Distribution: wives 4, brothers 12.

فإن تساوت الأعداد : أجزأ أحدهما عن الآخر، كامرأتين، وأخوين، فاضرب الاثنين في أصل المسألة. فإن كان أحد العددين جزءاً من الآخر : أغنى الأكثر عن الأقل، كأربع نسوة، وأخوَيْن، إذا ضَرَبْتَ الأربعة : أجزاك عن الأخوين. وإن وافق أحدُ العددَيْن الآخرَ : ضَرَبْتَ وَفْقَ أحدهما في جميع الآخر، ثم ما اجتمع : في أصل المسألة. * قوله: (فإن تساوت الأعداد أجزأ أحدهما عن الآخر، كامرأتين وأخوين، فاضرب اثنين في أصل المسألة). وهذا يُسمى المتماثل، فأصلها من أربعة للزوجتين: الربع سهم منكسر عليهما، وللأخوين ما بقي وهو ثلاثة منكسر أيضاً، وأحد العددين يُغنيك عن الآخر، فاضرِب اثنين في أربعة يكون ثمانية: للزوجتين سهمان، وللأخوين ستة. قوله: (وإن كان أحد العددين جزءاً من الآخر: أجزأ الأكثر عن الأقل، كأربع نسوة وأخوين : إذا ضربت الأربعة: أجزاك عن عدد الأخوين). وهذا يسمى المتداخل فنقول : أصلُ المسألة من أربعة للزوجات: سهم منكسر عليهن وللأخوين ثلاثةٌ منكسرة أيضاً، فاستغن بضرب الأربعة؛ لأن الاثنين يدخلان فيها، فاضرِب الأربعة في أربعة : فتكون ستة عشر للزوجات: أربعة، وللأخوين اثنا عشر. قوله: (فإن وافق أحد العددين الآخر: ضربت وَفْقَ أحدهما في جميع الآخر، فما اجتمع فاضربه في أصل المسألة. كأربع نسوة، وأختِ، وستة أعمام : فالستة توافق الأربعة بالنصف، فاضرب نصف أحدهما في جميع الآخر، ثم ما اجتمع في أصل المسألة : يكون ثمانية وأربعين، ومنها تصح.
If the numbers are equal: one suffices for the other, like two wives and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the case. If one number is a part of the other: the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers, if you multiply four, it suffices for the brothers. If one number agrees (is compatible) with the other: multiply the half of one by the whole of the other, then what results [is multiplied] by the base of the case. * His statement: (If the numbers are equal one suffices for the other, like two wives and two brothers, so multiply two by the base of the case). This is called mutamathil. Its base is four: for the two wives a quarter, a share broken over them, and for the two brothers the remainder which is three also broken, and one of the numbers suffices you from the other, so multiply two by four, it becomes eight: for the two wives two shares, and for the two brothers six. His statement: (If one number is a part of the other: the greater suffices for the lesser, like four women and two brothers: if you multiply four, it suffices from the number of brothers). This is called mutadakhil, so we say: the base of the case is four: for the wives a share broken over them and for the brothers three broken as well, so you dispense with multiplying four because the two are included in it, so multiply four by four: it becomes sixteen: for the wives four, and for the brothers twelve. His statement: (If one number agrees with the other: multiply the half of one by the whole of the other, then what results multiply by the base of the case. Like four women, a sister, and six uncles: the six agrees with the four by half, so multiply half of one by the whole of the other, then what results [by] the base of the case: it becomes forty-eight, and from it [the case] is corrected.)
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining three mathematical rules for correcting the base number (tas'hih al-mas'alah) when the shares of multiple heirs do not divide evenly. These rules apply when there are multiple heirs in the same category (e.g., multiple wives or multiple brothers) and the number of heirs does not divide evenly into the share. The commentator expands on each case with examples. First, tamathul (equality): when the numbers of two groups of heirs are equal, such as two wives and two brothers, one number suffices for the other; the base is multiplied by that number. Second, tadakhul (inclusion): when one number is a multiple of the other, like four wives and two brothers, the larger number suffices, so multiply the base by four. Third, tawafuq (agreement): when the numbers share a common factor, like four wives and six uncles, use the reciprocal of one number multiplied by the other. All examples follow the standard Hanafi method of inheritance calculation.

Key Terms
المتماثل (al-mutamāthil): Two numbers that are equal.
المتداخل (al-mutadākhil): One number is a part (divisor) of the other, so the larger suffices for correction.
المتوافق (al-mutawāfiq): Two numbers share a common divisor greater than 1.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the tadakhul example with four wives and two brothers, what is the multiplier used to correct the base?
A) 2
B) 4
C) 6
D) 8

Key Takeaway
Tadakhul uses the larger number to correct the base when one heir group size divides the other.
